Final answer: {'final_items': [{'contents': 'Optimizing Queries Using 
Materialized Views:\nA Practical, Scalable Solution\nJonathan Goldstein and 
Per-Åke Larson\nMicrosoft Research, One Microsoft Way, Redmond, WA 
98052\n{jongold,palarson}@microsoft.com\nAbstract\nMaterialized views can 
provide massive improvements in query\nprocessing time, especially for 
aggregation queries over large ta-\nbles.\nTo realize this potential, the query 
optimizer must know\nhow and when to exploit materialized views. This paper 
presents a\nfast and scalable algorithm for determining whether part or all of 
a\nquery can be computed from materialized views and describes\nhow it can be 
incorporated in transformation-based optimizers.\nThe current version handles 
views composed of selections, joins\nand a final group-by. Optimization remains 
fully cost based, that\nis, a single “best” rewrite is not selected by heuristic
rules but\nmultiple rewrites are generated and the optimizer chooses the 
best\nalternative in the normal way. Experimental results based on 
an\nimplementation in Microsoft SQL Server show outstanding per-\nformance and 
scalability. Optimization time increases slowly with\nthe number of views but 
remains low even up to a thousand.\nKeywords\nMaterialized views, view matching,
query optimization.\n1. Introduction\nUsing materialized views to speed up query
processing is an old\nidea [10] but only in the last few years has the idea been
adopted\nin commercial database systems. Recent TPC-R benchmark re-\nsults and 
actual customer experiences show that query processing\ntime can be improved by 
orders of magnitude through judicious\nuse of materialized views. To realize the
potential of materialized\nviews, efficient solutions to three issues are 
required:\n• \nView design: determining what views to materialize, includ-\ning 
how to store and index them.\n• \nView maintenance: efficiently updating 
materialized views\nwhen base tables are updated.\n• \nView exploitation: making
efficient use of materialized\nviews to speed up query processing.\nThis paper 
deals with view exploitation in transformation-based\noptimizers. Conceptually, 
an optimizer generates all possible re-\nwritings of a query expression, 
estimates their costs, and chooses\nthe one with the lowest cost. A 
transformation-based optimizer\ngenerates rewritings by applying local 
transformation rules on\nsubexpressions of the query. Applying a rule produces 
substitute\nexpressions, equivalent to the original expression. View 
matching,\nthat is, computing a subexpression from materialized views, is 
one\nsuch transformation rule. The view-matching rule invokes a view-\nmatching 
algorithm that determines whether the original expres-\nsion can be computed 
from one or more of the existing material-\nized views and, if so, generates 
substitute expressions. The algo-\nrithm may be invoked many times during 
optimization, each time\non a different subexpression.\nThe main contributions 
of this paper are (a) an efficient view-\nmatching algorithm for views composed 
of selections, joins and a\nfinal group-by (SPJG views) and (b) a novel index 
structure (on\nview definitions, not view data) that quickly narrows the search 
to\na small set of candidate views on which view-matching is applied.\nThe 
version of the algorithm described here is limited to SPJG\nviews and produces 
single-view substitutes. However, these are\nnot inherent limitations of our 
approach; the algorithm and the in-\ndex structure can be extended to a broader 
class of views and sub-\nstitutes. We briefly discuss possible extensions but 
the details are\nbeyond the scope of this paper.\nOur view-matching algorithm is
fast and scalable. Speed is crucial\nbecause the view-matching algorithm may be 
called many times\nduring optimization of a complex query. We also wanted an 
algo-\nrithm able to handle thousands of views efficiently. Many data-\nbase 
systems contain hundreds, even thousands, of tables. Such\ndatabases may have 
hundreds of materialized views. Tools similar\nto that described in [1] can also
generate large numbers of views.\nA smart system might also cache and reuse 
results of previously\ncomputed queries. Cached results can be treated as 
temporary ma-\nterialized views, easily resulting in thousands of 
materialized\nviews. The algorithm was implemented in Microsoft SQL 
Server,\nwhich uses a transformation-based optimizer based on the Cas-\ncades 
framework [6]. Experiments show outstanding performance\nand scalability. 
Optimization time increases linearly with the\nnumber of views but remains low 
even up to a thousand.\nIntegrating view matching through the optimizer’s normal
rule\nmechanism provides important benefits. Multiple rewrites may 
be\ngenerated; some exploiting materialized views, some not. All re-\nwrites 
participate in the normal cost-based optimization, regard-\nless of whether they
make use of materialized views. Secondary\nindexes, if any, on materialized 
views are automatically consid-\nered. The optimization time may even be 
reduced. If a cheap plan\nusing materialized views is found early in the 
optimization proc-\ness, it tightens cost bounds resulting in more aggressive 
pruning.\nThe rest of the paper is organized as follows. Section 2 
describes\nthe class of materialized views supported and defines the problem\nto
be solved. Section 3 describes our algorithm for deciding if a\nquery expression
can be computed from a view. Section 4 intro-\nduces our index structure. 
Section 5 presents experimental results\nbased on our prototype implementation. 
Related work is discussed\nin section 6. Section 7 contains a summary and a 
brief discussion\nof possible extensions.\n2. Defining the problem\nSQL Server 
2000 supports materialized views. They are called in-\ndexed views because a 
materialized view may be indexed in mul-\ntiple ways. A view is materialized by 
creating a unique clustered\nPermission to make digital or hard copies of all or

that\ncopies are not made or distributed for profit or commercial ad-\nvantage
