p_name)\\nThe first statement creates the view v1. The phrase “with 
sche-\\nmabinding” is required for indexed views. A count_big column 
is\\nrequired in all aggregation views so deletions can be handled 
in-\\ncrementally (when the count becomes zero, the group is empty\\nand the row
must be deleted). Output columns defined by arithme-\\ntic or other expressions 
must be assigned names (using the AS\\nclause) so that they can be referred to. 
The second statement ma-\\nterializes the view and stores the result in a 
clustered index. Even\\nthough the statement specifies only the (unique) key of 
the view,\\nthe rows contain all output columns. The final statement creates 
a\\nsecondary index on the materialized view. \\x00As outlined in the 
introduction, a transformation-based optimizer\\ngenerates rewrites by 
recursively applying transformation rules on\\nrelational expressions. View 
matching is a transformation rule that\\nis invoked on 
select-project-join-group-by (SPJG) expressions.\\nFor each expression, we want 
to find every materialized view\\nfrom which the expression can be computed. In 
this paper, we re-\\nquire that the expression can be computed from the view 
alone.\\nThe following is the view-matching problem considered in 
this\\npaper.\\nView Matching with Single-View Substitutes: Given a 
rela-\\ntional expression in SPJG form, find all materialized (SPJG)\\nviews 
from which the expression can be computed and, for each\\nview found, construct 
a substitute expression equivalent to the\\ngiven expression.\\nNo restrictions 
are imposed on the overall query. Even though we\\nconsider only single-view 
substitutes, different views may be used\\nto evaluate different parts of a 
query. Whenever the optimizer\\nfinds a SPJG expression the view-matching rule 
is invoked. All\\nsubstitutes produced by view matching participate in 
cost-based\\noptimization in the normal way. Furthermore, any secondary 
in-\\ndexes defined on a materialized view will be considered automati-\\ncally 
in the same way as for base tables.\\nThe algorithm explained in this paper is 
limited to SPJG subex-\\npressions and single-table substitutes. However, this 
is not an in-\\nherent limitation of our approach. The algorithm can be 
extended\\nto a broader class of input and substitute expressions, for 
example,\\nexpressions containing unions, outer joins or aggregation 
with\\ngrouping sets.\\n3. Computing a query expression from a view\\nIn this 
section, we describe the tests applied to determine whether\\na query expression
can be computed from a view and, if it can,\\nhow to construct the substitute 
expression. The first subsection\\ndeals with join-select-project (SPJ) views 
and queries assuming\\nthat the view and the query reference the same tables. 
Views with\\nextra tables and views with aggregation are covered in 
separate\\nsubsections. There is no need to consider views with fewer 
tables\\nthan the query expression. Such views can only be used to com-\\npute a
subexpression of the query expression. The view-matching\\nrule will 
automatically be invoked on every subexpression.\\nOur algorithm exploits four 
types of constraints: not-null con-\\nstraints on columns, primary key 
constraints, uniqueness con-\\nstraints (either explicitly declared or implied 
by creating a unique\\nindex), and foreign key constraints. We assume that the 
selection\\npredicates of view and query expressions have been converted\\ninto 
conjunctive normal form (CNF). If not, we first convert them\\ninto CNF. We also
assume that join elimination has been per-\\nformed so query and view 
expressions contain no redundant ta-\\nbles. (The SQL Server optimizer does this
automatically.)\\n3.1 Join-select-project views and queries\\nFor a SPJ query 
expression to be computable from a view, the\\nview must satisfy the following 
requirements.\\n1.\\nThe view contains all rows needed by the query 
expression.\\nBecause we are considering only single-view substitutes, this\\nis
an obvious requirement. However, this is not required if\\nsubstitutes 
containing unions of views are considered.\\n2.\\nAll required rows can be 
selected from the view. Even if all\\nrequired rows exist in the view, we may 
not be able to extract\\nthem correctly. Selection is done by applying a 
predicate. If\\none of the columns required by the predicate is missing 
from\\nthe view output, the required rows cannot be selected.\\n3.\\nAll output 
expressions can be computed from the output of\\nthe view.\\n4.\\nAll output 
rows occur with the correct duplication factor.\\nSQL is based on bag semantics,
that is, a base table or the\\noutput of a SQL expression may contain duplicate 
rows.\\nHence, it is not sufficient that two expressions produce the\\nsame set 
of rows but any duplicate rows must also occur ex-\\nactly the same number of 
times.\\nEquivalences among columns play an important role in our tests\\nso we 
cover this topic first. We then discuss how we ensure that\\nthe requirements 
above are met, devoting a separate subsection to\\neach requirement.\\n3.1.1 
Column equivalence classes\\nLet W =P1 ∧P2 ∧… ∧Pn be the selection predicate (in
CNF) of a\\nSPJ expression. By collecting the appropriate conjuncts, we 
can\\nrewrite the predicate as W = PE∧PNE where PE contains column\\nequality 
predicates of the form (Ti. Cp = Tj.Cq) and PNE contains\\nall remaining 
conjuncts. Ti and Tj are tables, not necessarily dis-\\ntinct, and Cp and Cq are
column references.\\nSuppose we evaluate the SPJ expression by computing the 
Carte-\\nsian product of the tables, then applying the 
column-equality\\npredicates in PE, then applying the predicates in PNE, and 
finally\\ncomputing the expressions in the output list. After the 
column-\\n332\\n\\nequality predicates have been applied, some columns are 
inter-\\nchangeable in both the PNE predicates and the output columns.\\nThis 
ability to reroute column references among equivalent col-\\numns will be 
important later on.\\nKnowledge about column equivalences can be captured 
com-\\npactly by computing a set of equivalence classes based on the col-\\numn 
equality predicates in PE. An equivalence class is a set of\\ncolumns that are 
known to be equal. Computing the equivalence\\nclasses is straightforward. Begin
with each column of the tables\\nreferenced by the expression in a separate set.
Then loop through\\nthe column equality predicates in any order. For each (Ti.Cp
=\\nTj.Cq), find the set containing Ti.Cp and the set containing Tj.Cq. 
If\\nthey are in different sets merge the two sets, otherwise do nothing.\\nThe 
sets left at the end is the desired collection of equivalence\\nclasses, 
including trivial classes consisting of a single column.\\n3.1.2 Do all required
rows exist in the view?\\nAssume that the query expression and the view 
expression refer-\\nence the tables T1, T2,…, Tm. Let Wq denote the predicate in
the\\nwhere-clause of the query expression and Wv the predicate of the\\nview 
expression. Determining whether the view contains all rows\\nrequired by the 
query expression is, in principle, easy. All we\\nneed to show is that the 
output of the expression (select *\\nfrom T1,T2,…,Tm where Wq) produces a subset
of the out-\\nput of (select * from T1,T2, …,Tm where Wv) for all\\nvalid 
instances of tables T1, T2, …, Tm. This is guaranteed to hold\\nif Wq ⇒Wv, where
‘⇒’ denotes logical implication.\\nTherefore we need an algorithm to decide 
whether Wq ⇒Wv\\nholds. We rewrite the predicates as Wq = Pq,1∧Pq,2∧…∧Pq,m 
and\\nWv = Pv,1∧Pv,2∧…∧Pv,n. A simple conservative algorithm is to\\ncheck that 
every conjunct Pv,i in Wv, matches a conjunct Pv,i in Wq.\\nThere are several 
ways to decide whether two conjuncts match.\\nFor instance, the matching can be 
purely syntactic.This can be\\nimplemented by converting each conjunct into a 
string, i.e., the\\nSQL text version of the conjunct, and then matching the 
strings.\\nThe drawback with this approach is that even minor 
syntactic\\ndifferences result in different strings. For example, the two 
predi-\\ncates (A > B) and (B < A) would not match. To avoid this prob-\\nlem, 
we must interpret the predicates and exploit equivalences\\namong expressions. 
Exploiting commutativity is a good example,\\napplicable to many types of 
expressions: comparisons, addition,\\nmultiplication, and disjunction (OR).\\nWe
can design matching\\nfunctions at different levels of sophistication and 
complexity de-\\npending on how much knowledge about equivalences we 
build\\ninto the function. For example, a simple function might only 
un-\\nderstand that (A+B) = (B+A), while a more sophisticated function\\nmight 
also recognize that (A/2 + B/5)*10 = A*5 + B*2.\\nOur decision algorithm 
exploits knowledge about column equiva-\\nlences and column ranges. We first 
divide the predicates Wq and\\nWv into three components and write the 
implication test as\\n(PEq ∧PRq ∧PUq ⇒PEv ∧PRv ∧PUv).\\nPEq consists of all 
column equality predicates from the query, PRq\\ncontains range predicates, and 
PUq is the residual predicate con-\\ntaining all remaining conjuncts of Wq. Wv 
is divided similarly. A\\ncolumn-equality predicate is any atomic predicate of 
the form\\n(Ti.Cp = Tj.Cr), where Cp and Cr are column references. A 
range\\npredicate is any atomic predicate of the form (Ti.Cp op c) where c\\nis 
a constant and op is one of the operators “<”, “≤”, “=”, “≥”,\\n“>”. The 
implication test can be split into three separate tests:\\n(PEq ∧PRq ∧PUq ⇒PEv )
∧\\n(PEq ∧PRq ∧PUq ⇒PRv) ∧\\n(PEq ∧PRq ∧PUq ⇒PUv).\\nAn implication test can be 
strengthened by dropping conjuncts in\\nthe antecedent. (Expressed in formal 
terms, the formula (A⇒C)\\n⇒(AB⇒C) holds for arbitrary predicates A, B, C. In 
words, if we\\ncan deduce that A by itself implies C then certainly A and B 
to-\\ngether imply C.) Our final tests are strengthened versions of the\\nthree 
tests. To determine whether all rows required by a query ex-\\nist in the view, 
we apply the following three tests:\\n(PEq ⇒PEv )\\n(Equijoin subsumption 
test)\\n(PEq ∧PRq ⇒PRv)\\n(Range subsumption test)\\n(PEq ∧PUq ⇒PUv 
)\\n(Residual subsumption test)\\nThe first test is called the equijoin 
subsumption test because, in\\npractice, most column equality predicates come 
from equijoins.\\nHowever, all column equality predicates are included in PE, 
even\\nthose referencing columns in the same table. Recall that the 
predi-\\ncates in PEq are the column equality predicates used for comput-\\ning 
the query equivalence classes. Since PEq is in the antecedent\\nin the latter 
two implications, we can reroute a column reference\\nto any column within its 
query equivalence class.\\nThe tests are clearly stronger than minimally 
required and may\\ncause some opportunities to be missed. For instance, by 
dropping\\nPRq from the antecedent of the equijoin test we will miss 
cases\\nwhen the query equates two columns to the same constant, 
say,\\n(A=2)∧(B=2) and the view contains the weaker predicate (A=B).\\nA similar
problem may arise in the residual subsumption test. For\\ninstance, if the query
contains (A=5)∧(B=3) and the view con-\\ntains the predicate (A+B) = 5, we would
safely but incorrectly\\nconclude that the view does not provide all required 
rows. It is a\\ntradeoff between speed and completeness.\\nCheck constraints can
be readily incorporated into the tests. The\\nkey observation is that check 
constraints on the tables of a query\\ncan be added to the where-clause without 
changing the query re-\\nsult. Hence, check constraints can be taken into 
account by in-\\ncluding them in the antecedent of the implication Wq 
⇒Wv.\\nWhether or not the check constraints will actually be exploited\\ndepends
on the algorithm used for testing.\\nEquijoin subsumption test.\\nThe equijoin 
subsumption test amounts to requiring that all col-\\numns equal in the view 
must also be equal in the query (but not\\nvice versa). We implement this test 
by first computing column\\nequivalence classes, as explained in the previous 
section, both for\\nthe query and the view, and then checking whether every 
non-\\ntrivial view equivalence class is a subset of some query equiva-\\nlence 
class. Just checking that all column equality predicates in\\nthe view also 
exist in the query is a much weaker test because of\\ntransitivity. Suppose the 
view contains ( A=B and B=C) and the\\nquery contains (A=C and C=B). Even though
the actual predi-\\ncates don’t match, they are logically equivalent because 
they both\\nimply that A=B=C. The effect of transitivity is correctly 
captured\\nby using equivalence classes.\\nIf the view passes the equijoin 
subsumption test, we know that it\\ndoes not contain any conflicting column 
equality constraints. We\\ncan also easily compute what, if any, compensating 
column equal-\\nity constraints must be enforced on the view to produce the 
query\\nresult. Whenever some view equivalence classes E1, E2 … En map\\nto the 
same query equivalence class E, we create a column-\\nequality predicate between
any column in Ei and any column in\\nEi+1 for i=1, 2, …, n-1.\\nRange 
subsumption test.\\nWhen no ORs are involved, there is an easy algorithm for 
the\\nrange subsumption test. We associate with each equivalence 
class\\n333\\n\\nin the query a range that specifies a lower and upper bound on 
the\\ncolumns in the equivalence class. Both bounds are initially 
left\\nuninitialized. We then consider the range predicates one by one,\\nfind 
the equivalence class containing the column referenced, and\\nset or adjust its 
range as needed. If the predicate is of type (Ti.Cp\\n<= c), we set the upper 
bound to the minimum of its current value\\nand c. If is of type (Ti.Cp >= c), 
we set the upper bound to the\\nmaximum of its current value and c. Predicates 
of the form (Ti.Cp\\n< c) are treated as (Ti.Cp <= c-∆) where c-∆denotes the 
smallest\\nvalue preceding c in the domain of column Ti.Cp. Predicates of 
the\\nform (Ti.Cp > c) are treated as (Ti.Cp <= c+∆). Finally, predicates\\nof 
the form (Ti.Cp = c) are treated as (Ti.Cp >= c)∧(Ti.Cp <= c).\\nThe same 
process is repeated for the view.\\nThe view cannot produce all required rows if
it is more tightly\\nconstrained than the query. To check this, we consider the 
view\\nequivalence classes with ranges where at least one of the bounds\\nhas 
been set. We find the matching equivalence class in the query,\\nthe query 
equivalence class that has at least one column in com-\\nmon with the query 
equivalence class, and check whether the\\nrange of the query equivalence class 
is contained in the range of\\nthe view equivalence class. (Uninitialized bounds
are treated as\\n+∞or -∞.) If it is not, the range subsumption test fails and 
the\\nview is rejected.\\nDuring this process we can determine what compensating
range\\npredicates must be applied to the view to produce the query result.\\nIf
a query range matches precisely the corresponding view range,\\nno restriction 
is needed. If the lower bound doesn’t match exactly,\\nwe must restrict the view
result by enforcing the predicate (T.C\\n>= lb) where T.C is a column in the 
(query) equivalence class and\\nlb is the lower bound of the query range. If the
upper bounds dif-\\nfer, we need to enforce the predicate (T.C <= ub).\\nThis 
range coverage algorithm can be extended to support dis-\\njunctions (OR) of 
range predicates. Due to space limitations, we\\nwill not discuss the extension 
here. Our prototype does not sup-\\nport disjunctions.\\nResidual subsumption 
test.\\nConjuncts that are neither column-equality predicates nor 
range\\npredicates form the residual predicates of the query and the view.\\nThe
only reasoning applied to these predicates is column equiva-\\nlence. We test 
the implication by checking whether every con-\\njunct in the view residual 
predicate matches a conjunct in the\\nquery residual predicate. Two column 
references match if they be-\\nlong to the same (query) equivalence class. If 
the match fails, the\\nview is rejected because the view contains a predicate 
not present\\nin the query. Any residual predicate in the query that did 
not\\nmatch anything in the view must be applied to the view.\\nAs discussed in 
the beginning of this section, whether two con-\\njuncts are found to match 
depends on the matching algorithm. Our\\nprototype implementation uses a shallow
matching algorithm: ex-\\ncept for column equivalences, the expressions must be 
identical.\\nAn expression is represented by a text string and a list of 
column\\nreferences. The text string contains the textual version of the 
ex-\\npression with column references omitted. The list contains every\\ncolumn 
reference in the expression, in the order they would occur\\nin the textual 
version of the expression. To compare two expres-\\nsions, we first compare the 
strings. If they are equal, we scan\\nthrough the two lists comparing column 
references in the same\\npositions in the two lists. If both column references 
are contained\\nin the same (query) equivalence class, the column 
references\\nmatch, otherwise not. If all column pairs match, the 
expressions\\nmatch. We chose this shallow algorithm for speed, fully 
aware\\nthat it may cause some opportunities to be missed.\\nIn summary, here 
are the steps of our procedure for testing\\nwhether a view contains all the 
rows needed by the query.\\n1.\\nCompute equivalence classes for the query and 
the view.\\n2.\\nCheck that every view equivalence class is a subset of 
a\\nquery equivalence class. If not, reject the view\\n3.\\nCompute range 
intervals for the query and the view.\\n4.\\nCheck that every view range 
contains the corresponding\\nquery range. If not, reject the view.\\n5.\\nCheck 
that every conjunct in the residual predicate of the\\nview matches a conjunct 
in the residual predicate of the\\nquery. If not, reject the view.\\nExample 
2:\\nView:\\nCreate view V2 with schemabinding as\\nSelect l_orderkey, 
o_custkey, l_partkey,\\nl_shipdate, o_orderdate,\\nl_quantity*l_extendedprice as
gross_revenue\\nFrom dbo.lineitem, dbo.orders, dbo.part\\nWhere l_orderkey = 
o_orderkey\\nAnd l_partkey\\n= p_partkey\\nAnd p_partkey >= 150\\nAnd o_custkey 
>= 50 and o_custkey <= 500\\nAnd p_name like ‘%abc%’\\nQuery:\\nSelect 
l_orderkey, o_custkey, l_partkey,\\nl_quantity*l_extendedprice\\nFrom lineitem, 
orders, part\\nWhere l_orderkey = o_orderkey\\nAnd l_partkey\\n= p_partkey\\nAnd
l_partkey >= 150 and l_partkey <= 160\\nAnd o_custkey = 123\\nAnd o_orderdate = 
l_shipdate\\nAnd p_name like ‘%abc%’\\nAnd l_quantity*l_extendedprice > 
100\\nStep 1: Compute equivalence classes.\\nView equivalence classes: 
{l_orderkey, o_orderkey},\\n{l_partkey, p_partkey}, {o_orderdate}, 
{l_shipdate}\\nQuery equivalence classes: {l_orderkey, 
o_orderkey},\\n{l_partkey, p_partkey}, {o_orderdate, l_shipdate}\\nNot all 
trivial equivalence classes are shown; {o_orderdate} and\\n{l_shipdate} are 
included because they are needed later in the ex-\\nample.\\nStep 2: Check view 
equivalence class containment.\\nThe two non-trivial view equivalence classes 
both have exact\\nmatches among the query equivalence classes. The 
(trivial)\\nequivalence classes {o_orderdate} and {l_shipdate} map to the\\nsame
query equivalence class, which means that the substitute 
ex-\\npression\\nmust\\ncreate\\nthe\\ncompensating\\npredicate\\n(o_orderdate=l
_shipdate).\\nStep 3: Compute ranges.\\nView ranges:\\n{l_partkey, p_partkey} 
∈(150, +∞),\\n{o_custkey} ∈(50, 500)\\nQuery ranges:\\n{l_partkey, p_partkey} 
∈(150, 160),\\n{o_custkey} ∈(123, 123)\\nStep 4: Check query range 
containment.\\nThe range (150, 160) on {l_partkey, p_partkey} is contained 
in\\nthe corresponding view range. The upper bounds do not match so\\nwe have to
enforce the predicate ({l_partkey, p_partkey} <= 160).\\nThe range (123, 123) on
{o_custkey} is also contained in the cor-\\nresponding view range. The bounds 
don’t mach so we must en-\\n334\\n\\nforce the predicates (o_custkey >= 123) and
(o_custkey <= 123),\\nwhich can be simplified to (o_custkey = 123).\\nStep 5: 
Check match of view residual predicates.\\nView residual predicate:\\np_name 
like ‘%abc%’\\nQuery residual predicate:\\np_name like 
‘%abc%’,\\nl_quantity*l_extendedprice > 100\\nThe view has only one residual 
predicate, p_name like ‘%abc%’,\\nwhich also exists in the query. The extra 
residual predicate,\\nl_quantity*l_extendedprice > 100 must be enforced.\\nThe 
view passes all the tests so we conclude that it contains all the\\nrequired 
rows. The compensating predicates that must be 
applied\\nto\\nthe\\nview\\nare\\n(o_orderdate\\n=\\nl_shipdate),\\n({p_partkey,
\\nl_partkey}\\n<=\\n160),\\n(o_custkey\\n=\\n123),\\nand\\n(l_quantity*l_extend
edprice > 100.00). The notation {p_partkey,\\nl_partkey} in the second 
predicates means that we can choose ei-\\nther p_partkey or l_partkey. 
\\x003.1.3 Can the required rows be selected?\\nWe explained in the previous 
section how to determine the com-\\npensating predicates that must be enforced 
on the view to reduce\\nit to the correct set of rows. They are of three 
different types.\\n1.\\nColumn equality predicates obtained while comparing 
view\\nand query equivalence classes. In our example above, there\\nwas one 
predicate of this type: (o_orderdate = l_shipdate).\\n2.\\nRange predicates 
obtained while checking query ranges\\nagainst view ranges. There were two 
predicates of this type: ,\\n({p_partkey, l_partkey} <= 160) and (o_custkey = 
123).\\n3.\\nUnmatched residual predicates from the query. There was\\none 
predicate of this type: (l_quantity*l_extendedprice >\\n100).\\nAll compensating
predicates must be computable from the view’s\\noutput. We exploit equalities 
among columns by considering each\\ncolumn reference to refer to the equivalence
class containing the\\ncolumn, instead of referencing the column itself. The 
query\\nequivalence classes are used in all but one case, namely, the 
com-\\npensating column equality predicates (point one in the list 
above).\\nThese predicates were introduced precisely to enforce 
additional\\ncolumn equalities required by the query.\\nEach such 
predicate\\nmerges two view equivalence classes and, collectively, they 
make\\nthe view equivalence classes equal to the query equivalence\\nclasses. 
Hence, a column reference can be redirected to any col-\\numn within its view 
equivalence class but not within its query\\nequivalence class.\\nCompensating 
predicates of type 1 and type 2 above contain only\\nsimple column references. 
All we need to do is check whether at\\nleast one of the columns in the 
referenced equivalence class is an\\noutput column of the view and route the 
reference to that column.\\nCompensating predicates of type 3 may involve more 
complex\\nexpressions. In that case, it may be possible to evaluate the 
ex-\\npression even though some of the columns referenced cannot be\\nmapped to 
an output column of the view. For example, if\\nl_quantity*l_extendedprice is 
available as a view output column,\\nwe can still evaluate the predicate 
(l_quantity*l_extendedprice >\\n100) without the columns l_quantity and 
l_extendedprice. How-\\never, our prototype implementation ignores this 
possibility and\\nrequires that all columns referenced in compensating 
predicates\\nbe mapped to (simple) output columns of the view.\\nIn summary, we 
determine whether all rows required by the query\\ncan be correctly selected 
from a view as follows.\\n1.\\nConstruct compensating column equality predicates
while\\ncomparing view equivalence classes against query equiva-\\nlence classes
as described in the previous section. Try to map\\nevery column reference to an 
output column (using the view\\nequivalence classes). If this is not possible, 
reject the view.\\n2.\\nConstruct compensating range predicates by comparing 
col-\\numn ranges as described in the previous section. Try to map\\nevery 
column reference to an output column (using the query\\nequivalence classes). If
this is not possible, reject the view.\\n3.\\nFind the residual predicates of 
the query that are missing in\\nthe view. Try to map every column reference to 
an output\\ncolumn (using the query equivalence classes). If this is 
not\\npossible, reject the view.\\n3.1.4 Can output expressions be 
computed?\\nChecking whether all output expressions of the query can 
be\\ncomputed from the view is similar to checking whether the addi-\\ntional 
predicates can be computed correctly. If the output expres-\\nsion is a 
constant, just copy the constant to the output. If the out-\\nput expression is 
a simple column reference, check whether it can\\nbe mapped (using the query 
equivalence classes) to an output col-\\numn of the view. For other expressions,
we first check whether\\nthe view output contains exactly the same expression 
(taking into\\naccount column equivalences). If so, the output expression is 
just\\nreplaced by a reference to the matching view output column. If\\nnot, we 
check whether the expression’s source columns can all be\\nmapped to view output
columns, i.e. whether the complete ex-\\npression can be computed from (simple) 
output columns. If the\\nview fails these tests, the view is rejected.\\nThis 
algorithm will miss some cases. For instance, we do not con-\\nsider whether 
some part of an expression matches a view output\\nexpression. Neither do we 
consider the case when it can be de-\\nduced that a query column is constant 
because of constraints in\\nthe where-clause, possibly taking into account check
constraints\\non the column.\\n3.1.5 Do rows occur with correct duplication 
factor?\\nWhen the query and the view reference exactly the same tables,\\nthis 
condition is trivially satisfied if the view passes the previous\\ntests. The 
more interesting case occurs when the view references\\nadditional tables, which
is covered in the next section.\\n3.2 Views with extra tables\\nSuppose we have 
a SPJ query that references tables T1, T2 ,…, Tn\\nand a view that references 
one additional table, that is, tables T1,\\nT2 ,…, Tn, S. Under what 
circumstances can the query still be\\ncomputed from the view? Our approach is 
based on recognizing\\ncardinality-preserving joins (sometimes called table 
extension\\njoins). A join between tables T and S is cardinality preserving 
if\\nevery row in T joins with exactly one row in S. If so, we can view\\nS as 
simply extending T with the columns from S. An equijoin be-\\ntween all columns 
in a non-null foreign key in T and a unique key\\nin S has this property. A 
foreign key constraint guarantees that,\\nfor every row t of T, there exists at 
least one row s in S with\\nmatching column values for all non-null foreign-key 
columns in t.\\nAll columns in t containing a null are ignored when validating 
the\\nforeign-key constraint.\\nIt can be shown that all 
requirements\\n(equijoin, all columns, non-null, foreign key, unique key) are 
es-\\nsential.\\nNow consider the case when the view references multiple 
extra\\ntables. Suppose the query references tables T1, T2 ,…, Tn and the\\nview
references m extra tables, that is, it references tables T1, T2\\n,…, Tn , Tn+1,
Tn+2 ,…, Tn+m. To determine whether tables Tn+1,\\n335\\n\\nTn+2 ,…, Tn+m are 
joined to tables T1, T2 ,…, Tn through a series of\\ncardinality preserving 
joins we build a directed graph, called the\\nforeign-key join graph. The nodes 
in the graph represent tables T1,\\nT2 ,…, Tn , Tn+1, Tn+2 ,…, Tn+m. There is an
edge from table Ti to\\ntable Tj if the view specifies, directly or 
transitively, a join be-\\ntween tables Ti and Tj and the join satisfies all the
five require-\\nments listed above (equijoin, all columns, non-null, foreign 
key,\\nunique key). To capture transitive equijoin conditions correctly\\nwe 
must use the equivalence classes when adding edges to the\\ngraph. Suppose we 
are considering whether to add an edge from\\ntable Ti to table Tj and there is 
an acceptable foreign key con-\\nstraint going from columns F1, F2, …, Fn of 
table Ti to columns\\nC1, C2, …, Cn of Tj. For each column Ci, we locate the 
column’s\\nequivalence class and check whether the corresponding foreign\\nkey 
column Fi is part of the same equivalence class. If the join\\ncolumns pass this
test, we add the edge.\\nOnce the graph has been built, we try to eliminate 
nodes Tn+1, Tn+2\\n,…, Tn+m by a sequence of deletions. We repeatedly delete 
any\\nnode that has no outgoing edges and exactly one incoming 
edge.\\n(Logically, this performs the join represented by the incoming\\nedge.) 
When a node Ti is deleted, its incoming edge is also de-\\nleted, which may make
another node deletable. This process con-\\ntinues until no more nodes can be 
deleted or the nodes Tn+1, Tn+2\\n,…, Tn+m have been eliminated. If we succeed 
in eliminating nodes\\nTn+1, Tn+2 ,…, Tn+m, the extra tables in the view can be 
eliminated\\nthrough cardinality-preserving joins and the view passes this 
test.\\nThe view must still pass the tests detailed in the previous 
section\\n(subsumption tests, required output columns available). 
However,\\nthese test all assume that the query and the view reference 
the\\nsame tables. To make them the same, we conceptually add the ex-\\ntra 
tables Tn+1, Tn+2 ,…, Tn+m to the query and join them to the ex-\\nisting tables
T1, T2 ,…, Tn through exactly the same foreign-key\\njoins that were used to 
eliminate them from the view. Because the\\njoins are all cardinality 
preserving, this will not change the result\\nof the query in any way. In 
practice, we merely simulate the addi-\\ntion of extra tables by updating query 
equivalence classes. We\\nfirst add a trivial equivalence class for each column 
in tables Tn+1,\\nTn+2 ,…, Tn+m. (We have now added the tables to the from 
clause\\nof the query.) Next, we scan the join conditions of all 
foreign-key\\nedges deleted during the elimination process above and 
apply\\nthem to query equivalence classes. This will cause some 
query\\nequivalence classes to merge. (We have now added the join condi-\\ntions
to the where-clause.) At the end of this process, the (concep-\\ntually) 
modified query references the same tables as the view and\\nthe query 
equivalence classes have been updated to reflect this\\nchange. After this 
modification, all tests described in the previous\\nsection can be applied 
unchanged.\\nExample 3: This example illustrates views with extra 
tables.\\nView:\\nCreate view v3 with schemabinding as\\nSelect c_custkey, 
c_name, l_orderkey,\\nl_partkey, l_quantity\\nFrom dbo.lineitem, dbo.orders, 
dbo.customer\\nWhere l_orderkey = o_orderkey\\nAnd o_custkey = c_custkey\\nAnd 
o_orderkey >= 500\\nQuery:\\nSelect l_orderkey, l_partkey, l_quantity\\nFrom 
lineitem\\nWhere l_orderkey between 1000 and 1500\\nAnd l_shipdate = 
l_commitdate\\nWe obtain the following equivalence classes and ranges for 
the\\nview and the query.\\nView:\\n{l_orderkey, o_orderkey}, {o_custkey, 
c_custkey}\\n{ l_orderkey, o_orderkey} ∈(500, +∞)\\nQuery:\\n{l_shipdate, 
l_commitdate}\\n{l_orderkey} ∈(1000, 1500)\\nThe foreign-key join graph for the 
view consists of three nodes\\n(lineitem, orders, customer) with an edge from 
lineitem to orders\\nand an edge from orders to customer. The customer node can 
be\\ndeleted because it has no outgoing edges and one incoming edge.\\nThis also
deletes the edge from orders to customer. Now orders\\nhas no outgoing edges and
can be removed.\\nWe then conceptually add orders and customer to the query. 
The\\njoin predicate for the lineitem-to-orders edge is l_orderkey 
=\\no_orderkey, which generates the equivalence class 
{l_orderkey,\\no_orderkey}. The join predicate for the orders-to-customer edge 
is\\no_custkey = c_custkey, which generates the equivalence class\\n{o_custkey, 
c_custkey}. The updated query equivalence classes\\nand ranges for the query 
are\\nQuery:\\n{l_shipdate, l_commitdate}, {l_orderkey, 
o_orderkey},\\n{o_custkey, c_custkey};\\n{ l_orderkey, o_orderkey} ∈(1000, 
1500)\\nWe then apply the subsumption tests. The view passes the equi-\\njoin 
subsumption test because every view equivalence class is a\\nsubset of a query 
equivalence class. It also passes the range sub-\\nsumption test because the 
view range { l_orderkey, o_orderkey}\\n∈(500, +∞) contains the corresponding 
query range { l_orderkey,\\no_orderkey} ∈(1000, 1500). The compensating 
predicates are\\nl_orderkey >= 1000 and l_orderkey <= 1500, which can be 
en-\\nforced because l_orderkey is available in the view output.\\nFi-\\nnally, 
every output column of the view can be computed from the\\nview output. \\x00The
procedure above ensures that we can “prejoin”, directly or in-\\ndirectly, each 
extra table in the view to some input table T of the\\nquery and the resulting, 
wider table will contain exactly the same\\nrows as T. This is safe but somewhat
restrictive because we only\\nneed to guarantee it for the rows actually 
consumed by the query,\\nnot all rows. Here is an example of such a case. 
Suppose we have\\na view consisting of tables T and S joined on T.F=S.C where F 
is\\ndeclared as a foreign key referencing C and C is the primary key\\nof S. 
Now consider a selection query on table T with the predicate\\nT.F > 50. If T.F 
is not declared with “not null”, the view will be\\nrejected by our procedure. 
The join of T and S does not preserve\\nthe cardinality of T because rows with a
null in column T.F are\\nnot present in the view. However, for the subset of 
rows with a\\nnon-null T.F value, it does preserve cardinality, which is all 
that\\nmatters because of the null-rejecting predicate T.F > 50 in the\\nquery. 
On other words, any row in T containing a null value in\\nT.F will be discarded 
the query predicate in any case. The algo-\\nrithm can be modified to handle 
this case (not yet implemented).\\nAll that is required is an additional check 
when considering\\nwhether to add an edge to the foreign-key join graph. A 
foreign\\nkey column allowing nulls is still acceptable if the query 
contains\\na null-rejecting predicate on the column (other than the 
equijoin\\npredicate).\\n3.3 Aggregation queries and views\\nIn this section, we
consider aggregation queries and views. SQL\\nServer allows expressions, as 
opposed to just columns, in the\\ngroup-by list both in queries and materialized
views. In a materi-\\nalized view, all group-by expressions must also be in the 
output\\n336\\n\\nlist to ensure a unique key for each row. In addition, the 
output list\\nmust contain a count_big(*) column so deletions can be 
handled\\nincrementally. The only other aggregation function currently 
al-\\nlowed in materialized views is sum.\\nWe treat aggregation queries as 
consisting of a SPJ query fol-\\nlowed by a group-by operation and similarly for
views. An aggre-\\ngation query can be computed from a view if the view 
satisfies the\\nfollowing requirements.\\n1.\\nThe SPJ part of the view produces
all rows needed by the\\nSPJ part of the query and with the right duplication 
factor.\\n2.\\nAll columns required by compensating predicates (if any) 
are\\navailable in the view output.\\n3.\\nThe view contains no aggregation or 
is less aggregated than\\nthe query, i.e, the groups formed by the query can be 
com-\\nputed by further aggregation of groups output by the view.\\n4.\\nAll 
columns required to perform further grouping (if neces-\\nsary) are available in
the view output.\\n5.\\nAll columns required to compute output expressions 
are\\navailable in the view output.\\nThe first two requirements are the same as
for SPJ queries. The\\nthird requirement is satisfied if the group-by list of 
the query is a\\nsubset of the group-by list of the view. That is, if the view 
is\\ngrouped on expressions A, B, C then the query can be grouped on\\nany 
subset of A, B, C. This includes the empty set, which corre-\\nsponds to an 
aggregation query without a group-by clause.\\nWe\\ncurrently require that each 
group-by expression in the query\\nmatch exactly some group-by expression in the
view (taking into\\naccount column equivalences). This can be relaxed. As shown 
in\\n[16], it is sufficient that the grouping expression of the view 
func-\\ntionally determine the grouping expressions of the query.\\nIf the query
group-by list is equal to the view group-by list, no\\nfurther aggregation is 
needed so the fourth requirement is auto-\\nmatically satisfied. If it is a 
strict subset, then we must add a com-\\npensating group-by on top of the view. 
The grouping expressions\\nare the same as for the query. Because they are a 
subset of the\\nview grouping expressions and all grouping expressions of 
the\\nview must be part of the view output, they are always available in\\nthe 
view output. Hence, the third requirement is automatically 
sat-\\nisfied.\\nTesting whether the fourth requirement is satisfied is 
virtually\\nidentical to what was discussed for SPJ queries. The only 
slight\\ndifference occurs when dealing with aggregation expressions. If\\nthe 
query specifies a count(*) and the view is an aggregation\\nview, the count(*) 
must be replaced by a SUM over the view’s\\ncount_big(*) column.\\nIf the query 
output contains a SUM(E)\\nwhere E is some scalar expression, we require that 
the view con-\\ntain an output column that matches exactly (taking into 
account\\ncolumn\\nequivalences).\\nAVG(E)\\nis\\nconverted\\nto\\nSUM(E)/count_
big(*).\\nExample 4: This may sound too easy, perhaps causing some\\nreaders to 
wonder whether we handle situations illustrated by the\\nfollowing example view 
and query.\\nView:\\nCreate view v4 with schemabinding as\\nSelect o_custkey, 
count_big(*) as cnt\\nsum(l_quantity*l_extendedprice)as revenue\\nFrom 
dbo.lineitem, dbo.orders\\nWhere l_orderkey = o_orderkey\\nGroup by 
o_custkey\\nQuery:\\nSelect 
c_nationkey,\\nsum(l_quantity*l_extendedprice)\\nFrom lineitem, orders, 
customer\\nWhere l_orderkey = o_orderkey\\nAnd o_custkey = c_custkey\\nGroup by 
c_nationkey\\nIt is easy to see that the query can be computed from the view 
by\\njoining it with the customer table and then aggregating the result\\non 
c_nationkey. However, the view satisfies none of the condi-\\ntions listed above
so one might conclude that we will miss this\\nopportunity. Not so – this is a 
case where integration with the\\noptimizer helps. For queries of this type, the
SQL Server opti-\\nmizer also generates alternatives that include 
preaggregation. That\\nis, it will generate the following form of the query 
expression.\\nSelect c_nationkey, sum(rev)\\nFrom customer,\\n(select 
o_custkey,\\nsum(l_quantity*l_extendedprice) as rev\\nFrom lineitem, 
orders\\nWhere l_orderkey = o_orderkey\\nGroup by o_custkey) as iq\\nWhere 
c_custkey = o_custkey\\nGroup by c_nationkey\\nWhen the view-matching algorithm 
is invoked on the inner query,\\nit easily recognizes that the expression can be
computed from v4.\\nSubstituting in the view then produces exactly the desired 
expres-\\nsion, namely,\\nSelect c_nationkey, sum(revenue)\\nFrom customer, 
v4\\nWhere c_custkey = o_custkey\\nGroup by c_nationkey\\n4. Fast filtering of 
views\\nTo speed up view matching we maintain in memory a description\\nof every
materialized view. The view descriptions contain all in-\\nformation needed to 
apply the tests described in the previous sec-\\ntion. Even so, it is slow to 
apply the tests to all views each time\\nthe view-matching rule is invoked if 
the number of views is very\\nlarge. In this section, we describe an in-memory 
index, called a\\nfilter tree, which allows us to quickly discard views that 
cannot be\\nused by a query.\\nA filter tree is a multiway search tree where all
the leaves are on\\nthe same level. A node in the tree contains a collection of 
(key,\\npointer) pairs. A key consists of a set of values, not just a 
single\\nvalue. A pointer in an internal node points to a node at the 
next\\nlevel while a pointer in a leaf node points to a list of view 
descrip-\\ntions. A filter tree subdivides the set of views into smaller 
and\\nsmaller partitions at each level of the tree.\\nA search in a filter tree 
may traverse multiple paths. When the\\nsearch reaches a node, it continues 
along some of the node’s out-\\ngoing pointers. Whether to continue along a 
pointer is determined\\nby applying a search condition on the key associated 
with the\\npointer. The condition is always of the same type: a key 
qualifies\\nif it is a subset (superset) of or equal to a given search key. 
The\\nsearch key is also a set. We can always do a linear scan and check\\nevery
key but this may be slow if the node contains many keys.\\nTo avoid a linear 
scan, we organize the keys in a lattice structure,\\nwhich allows us to find all
subsets (supersets) of a given search\\nkey easily. We call this internal 
structure a lattice index.\\nWe describe the lattice index in more detail in the
next section\\nand then explain the partitioning conditions applied at 
different\\nlevels of the tree.\\n337\\n\\n4.1 Lattice index\\nThe subset 
relationship between sets imposes a partial order\\namong sets, which can be 
represented as a lattice. As the name in-\\ndicates, a lattice index organizes 
the keys in a graph that corre-\\nspond to the lattice structure. In addition to
the (key, downward\\npointer) pair described above, a node in the lattice index 
contains\\ntwo collections of pointers, superset pointers and subset 
pointers.\\nA superset pointer of a node V points to a node that represents 
a\\nminimal superset of the set represented by V. Similarly, a subset\\npointer 
of V points to a node that represents a maximal subset of\\nthe set represented 
by V. Sets with no subsets are called roots and\\nsets without supersets are 
called tops. A lattice index also con-\\ntains an array of pointers to tops and 
an array of pointers to roots.\\nFigure 1 shows a lattice index storing eight 
key sets.\\nABC\\nABF\\nBCDE\\nBE\\nAB\\nA\\nB\\nTops\\nRoots\\nD\\nFigure 1: 
Lattice index storing eight key sets:\\nA, B, D, AB, BE, ABC, ABF, 
BCDE\\nSearching a lattice index is a simple recursive procedure. Suppose\\nwe 
want to find supersets of AB. We start from the top nodes,\\nwhere we find that 
ABC and ABF are supersets of AB. From each\\nqualifying node, we recursively 
follow the subset pointers, at each\\nstep checking whether the target node is 
subsets of AB. AB is ac-\\nceptable but none if its subset nodes are. The search
returns ABC,\\nABF, and AB. Note that AB is reached twice, once from ABC 
and\\nonce from ABF. To avoid visiting and possibly returning the same\\nnode 
multiple times, the search procedure must remember which\\nnodes have been 
visited.\\nThe algorithm for finding subsets is\\nsimilar; the main difference 
is that the search starts from root\\nnodes and proceeds upwards following 
superset pointers.\\nDue to space constraints, we will not describe insertion 
and dele-\\ntion algorithms for lattice indexes. They are not complex but 
some\\ncare is required with the details.\\n4.2 Partitioning conditions\\nA 
filter tree recursively subdivides the set of views into smaller\\nand smaller 
non-overlapping partitions. At each level, a different\\npartitioning condition 
is applied. In this section, we describe the\\npartition conditions used in our 
prototype.\\n4.2.1 Source table condition\\nViews that lack some of the tables 
required by the query can be\\ndiscarded. This in captured by the following 
condition.\\nSource table condition: A query cannot be computed from a\\nview 
unless the view’s set of source tables is a superset of the\\nquery’s set of 
source tables.\\nWe build a lattice index using the view’s set of source tables 
as\\nthe key. Given a query’s set of source tables, we search the 
lattice\\nindex for partitions containing views that satisfy this 
condition.\\n4.2.2 Hub condition\\nRecall the algorithm explained in section 3.2
for eliminating extra\\ntables from a view. In that section, it was sufficient 
to have the al-\\ngorithm reduce the set of source tables to the same set as 
that of\\nthe query. However, we can let the algorithm run until no 
further\\ntables can be eliminated from the view, thereby reducing the 
re-\\nmaining set of tables to the smallest set possible. We call the 
re-\\nmaining set the hub of the view. As discussed in section 3.2, a\\nview 
cannot be used to answer a query unless we can eliminate all\\nextra tables 
through cardinality-preserving joins. The hub cannot\\nbe reduced further so 
clearly we can disregard any view whose\\nhub is not a subset of the query’s set
of source tables. This obser-\\nvation gives us the following condition.\\nHub 
condition: A query cannot be computed from a view unless\\nthe hub of the view 
is a subset of the query’s set of source tables.\\nThe previous condition gave 
us a lower bound on a view’s set of\\nsource tables while this condition gives 
us an upper bound. We\\nagain use a lattice index structure but this time with 
view hubs as\\nthe key instead of the complete set of source tables.\\nGiven 
a\\nquery, we then search the index for nodes whose key is a subset of\\nthe 
query’s set of source tables.\\nThe algorithm outlined above for computing view 
hubs tends to\\nproduce hubs that are unnecessarily small because it takes into 
ac-\\ncount only foreign-key joins. It can be improved by also taking\\ninto 
account other predicates. Suppose T is a table that would be\\neliminated from 
the hub by the algorithm above.\\nLet T.C be a\\ncolumn not participating in any
non-trivial equivalence class. If\\nT.C is referenced in a range or other 
predicate, we can leave T in\\nthe hub. The join is no longer guaranteed to be 
cardinality pre-\\nserving because the predicate on T.C may reject some rows in 
T.\\nThe only way the reference to T.C can be rerouted to another col-\\numn is 
if the query contains a column equality predicate involving\\nT.C. However, if 
that is the case, then T must also be among the\\nquery’s source tables so 
leaving T in the hub will not cause the\\nview to be rejected.\\n4.2.3 Output 
column condition\\nAssume for the moment that the output lists of queries and 
views\\nare all simple column references. We will deal with more 
complex\\noutput expressions separately. As stated earlier, a query cannot 
be\\ncomputed from a view unless all its output expressions can be\\ncomputed 
from the output of the view. However, this does not\\nmean that a query output 
column has to match an output column\\nbecause of equivalences among columns. 
The following example\\nillustrates what is required.\\nExample 6:\\nSuppose the
query outputs columns A, B, and C. Furthermore,\\nsuppose the query contains 
column equality predicates generating\\nthe following equivalence classes: {A, 
D, E}, {B, F} {C}. The\\ncolumns within an equivalence class all have the same 
value so\\nthey can be used interchangeably in the output list. We 
indicate\\nthis choice by writing the output list as {A, D, E}, {B, F} 
{C}.\\nNow consider a view that outputs {A, D, G}, {E}, {B} and {C,\\nH} where 
the column equivalences have been computed from the\\ncolumn equality predicates
of the view. The columns that are ac-\\ntually included in the output list are 
indicated by underlining.\\nLogically, we can then treat the view as outputting 
all of the col-\\numns, that is, as if its output list were extended to A, D, G,
E, B,\\nC, H.\\nThe first output column of the query can be computed from 
the\\nview if at least one of the columns A, D, or E exists in the 
view’s\\nextended output list. In this case, all of them do. Similarly, 
the\\nsecond column can be computed if B or F exists in the extended\\noutput 
list. B does (but F does not). Finally, the third output col-\\numn requires C, 
which also exists in the extended output list.\\nConsequently, the query output 
can be computed from the view. \\x00338\\n\\nAs this example illustrated, to 
correctly test availability of output\\ncolumns we must take into account column
equivalences in the\\nquery and in the view. We do this by replacing each column
refer-\\nence in the output list by a reference to the columns 
equivalence\\nclass. For a view, we then compute the extended output list by 
in-\\ncluding every column in the referenced equivalence classes. We\\ncan now 
state the condition that must hold.\\nOutput column condition: A view cannot 
provide all required\\noutput columns unless, for each equivalence class in the 
query’s\\noutput list, at least one of its columns is available in the 
view’s\\nextended output list.\\nTo exploit this condition, we build lattice 
indexes using the ex-\\ntended output lists of the views as keys. Given a query,
we search\\nthe index recursively beginning from the top nodes. A node 
quali-\\nfies if its extended output list satisfies the condition above. If 
so,\\nwe follow its subset pointers. If not, it is not necessary to follow\\nthe
pointers because, if a view V does not provide all required\\ncolumns, neither 
does any view whose extended output list is a\\nsubset of V’s extended output 
list.\\n4.2.4 Grouping columns\\nAn aggregation query cannot be computed from an
aggregation\\nview unless the query’s grouping columns are a subset of 
the\\nview’s grouping columns, again taking into account column\\nequivalences. 
This is exactly the same relationship as the one that\\nmust hold between the 
output columns of the query and the view.\\nConsequently, we can extend the 
views’ grouping lists in the same\\nway as for output columns and build lattice 
indexes on the ex-\\ntended grouping lists. For completeness, here is the 
condition that\\nmust hold between grouping columns.\\nGrouping column 
condition: An aggregation query cannot be\\ncomputed from an aggregation view 
unless, for each equivalence\\nclass in the query’s grouping list, at least one 
of its columns is\\npresent in the view’s extended grouping list.\\n4.2.5 Range 
constrained columns\\nA query cannot be computed from a view that specifies 
range\\nconstraints on a column that is not range constrained in the 
view,\\nagain taking into account column equivalences. We associate with\\neach 
query and view a range constraint list, where each entry ref-\\nerences a column
equivalence class. A column equivalence class\\nis included in the list if it 
has a constrained range, that is, at least\\none of the bounds has been set. 
Next, we compute an extended\\nconstraint list in the same way as for output 
columns but this time\\nfor the query but not the view. We can now state the 
condition\\nthat must hold.\\nRange constraint condition: A query cannot be 
computed from a\\nview unless, for each equivalence class in the view’s range 
con-\\nstraint list, at least one of its columns is present in the query’s 
ex-\\ntended range constraint list.\\nNote that the extended range constraint 
list is associated with the\\nquery, and not the view. Hence, lattice indexes on
the extended\\nconstraint lists of views, mimicking the indexes on output 
col-\\numns and grouping columns, cannot be used. However, we can\\nbuild 
lattice indexes based on a weaker condition involving a re-\\nduced range 
constraint list. The reduced range constraint list con-\\ntains only columns 
that reference trivial equivalence classes, i.e.\\ncolumns that are not 
equivalent to any other columns.\\nWeak range constraint condition: A query 
cannot be computed\\nfrom a view unless the view’s reduced range constraint list
is a\\nsubset of the query’s extended range constraint list.\\nWhen building a 
lattice index based on this condition, the com-\\nplete constraint list of a 
view is included and used as the key of a\\nnode but the subset-superset 
relationship is computed solely based\\non reduced constraint lists. A search 
starts from the roots and pro-\\nceeds upwards along superset edges. If a node 
passes the weak\\nrange constraint condition, we follow its superset pointers 
but the\\nnode is returned only if it also passes the range constraint. If 
a\\nnode fails the weak range constraint condition, all of its superset\\nnodes 
will also fail so there is no need to check them.\\n4.2.6 Residual predicate 
condition\\nRecall that we treat all predicates that are neither 
column-equality\\npredicates nor range predicates as residual predicates. The 
resid-\\nual subsumption test checks that every residual predicate in the\\nview
also exists in the query, again taking into account column\\nequivalences. Our 
implementation of the test uses a matching\\nfunction that compares predicates 
converted to text strings, omit-\\nting column references, and then matches 
column references sepa-\\nrately. We associate with each view and query, a 
residual predi-\\ncate list containing just the text strings of the residual 
predicates.\\nThen the following condition must hold.\\nResidual predicate 
condition: A query cannot be computed from\\na view unless the view’s residual 
predicate list is a subset of the\\nquery’s residual predicate list.\\nFor 
filtering purposes, we then build lattice indexes using the re-\\nsidual 
predicate lists of the views as keys. Given a query’s resid-\\nual list, we 
search for nodes whose key is a subset of the query’s\\nresidual list.\\n4.2.7 
Output expression condition\\nOutput expressions are handled in much the same 
way as residual\\npredicates. We convert the expressions to text strings, 
omitting\\ncolumn references, and associate with each view and query an\\noutput
expression list consisting of the text strings of its output\\nexpressions. We 
can then build lattice indexes based on the fol-\\nlowing condition.\\nOutput 
expression condition: A query cannot be computed from\\na view unless its 
(textual) output expression list is a subset of the\\nview’s (textual) output 
expression list.\\nA search then looks for nodes whose key is a superset of 
the\\nquery’s (textual) output expression list. The condition is 
conserva-\\ntive in the sense that we ignore the possibility of computing 
an\\nexpression from “scratch” using plain columns or precomputed\\nparts. The 
condition can be weakened to cover this possibility but\\nthe details are beyond
the scope of this paper.\\n4.2.8 Grouping expression condition\\nExpressions in 
the grouping clause can be handled in the same\\nway as expressions in the 
output list. For completeness, we state\\nthe condition.\\nGrouping expression 
condition: An aggregation query cannot be\\ncomputed from an aggregation view 
unless its (textual) grouping\\nexpression list is a subset of the view’s 
(textual) grouping expres-\\nsion list.\\n4.3 Summary\\nAs we saw, each 
condition above can be the basis for a lattice in-\\ndex subdividing a 
collection of views. The conditions are inde-\\npendent and can be composed in 
any order to create a filter tree.\\nFor instance, we can create a filter tree 
where the root node parti-\\ntions the views based on their hubs and the second 
level nodes\\nfurther subdivide each partition according to the views’ 
extended\\n339\\n\\noutput column lists. We can stop there or add more levels 
using\\nany other conditions. Our implementation uses a filter tree with\\neight
levels. From top to bottom, the levels are: hubs, source ta-\\nbles, output 
expressions, output columns, residual constraints, and\\nrange constraints. For 
aggregation views, there are two additional\\nlevels: grouping expressions and 
grouping columns.\\n5. Experimental results\\nWe have implemented the 
view-matching algorithm, including the\\nfilter tree, in SQL Server. One goal 
was to keep optimization time\\nlow, even when the number of materialized views 
is high. We ran\\na series of experiments that measured the increase in 
optimization\\ntime and the benefits of using the filter tree.\\nThe views used 
in the experiments were randomly generated\\nSPJG views over the TPC-H/R 
database. Each view was gener-\\nated by randomly selecting a table and joining 
in additional tables\\nrandomly through foreign key equijoins. Then range 
predicates\\nwere added on randomly selected columns until the estimated 
car-\\ndinality of the SPJ part of the result was within 25-75% of the\\nlargest
table included. Output columns were also selected ran-\\ndomly. About 75% of the
views were aggregation views, using\\nrandomly selected output columns for 
grouping. Any remaining\\n(numerical) output column was used as the argument for
a SUM.\\nA parameter file specified the frequency with which a table 
was\\nchosen as the initial table, the frequency with which a foreign key\\nwas 
select for a join, the frequency with which a column received\\na range 
predicate, and the frequency with which a column was\\nchosen as an output 
column. We generated a total of 1000 materi-\\nalized views. Queries were 
generated in the same way but with a\\ndifferent seed for the random number 
generator and restricting the\\ncardinality of the result to within 8-12% of the
largest table used\\nin the query. 40% of the queries referenced two tables, 20%
ref-\\nerenced three, 17% referenced four, 13% referenced five, 8% 
ref-\\nerenced six, and 2% referenced seven tables.\\nAll experiments were run 
on a machine with 128 MB of memory\\nand a 700MHz Pentium processor. The 
database was TPC-H at\\nscale factor 0.5 (500MB) with primary keys and foreign 
keys de-\\nfined. The scale factor does not affect optimization time.\\nFigure 2
shows the total optimization time for 1000 randomly\\ngenerated queries varying 
the number of materialized views. The\\nbottom two lines were measured with the 
filter tree enabled. The\\nline labeled Alt&Filter shows the total optimization 
time. The op-\\ntimization time increases linearly with the number of views. 
For\\n1000 views, it increased by about 60%. The absolute time is still\\nlow; 
on average, only 0.15 seconds per query. The increase in op-\\ntimization time 
is caused by two factors: the time for finding and\\nchecking candidate views 
and the time spent in the optimizer\\nprocessing the substitutes produced. In an
attempt to separate the\\ntwo components, we also ran the optimization without 
introducing\\nsubstitutes. That is, the view-matching algorithm performed 
its\\nnormal analysis but always returned without producing substi-\\ntutes. The
bottom line shows the optimization time when no sub-\\nstitutes were produced. 
This shows that approximately 35% of the\\ntime was spent finding and checking 
views and the rest spent on\\nfurther processing of the substitutes 
produced.\\nThe top two lines (dashed) show the optimization time with 
the\\nfilter tree disabled. Using the filter tree to find candidate 
views\\nreduces the optimization time significantly. With 1000 views, 
the\\noptimization time increases by about 110% when the filter tree 
is\\ndisabled, as opposed to about 60% when it is enabled.\\nEstimating the time
spent on view matching simply by never pro-\\nducing substitutes is not entirely
accurate. Introducing substitutes\\nincreases the amount of time spent on view 
matching because the\\nsubstitutes will result in additional expressions being 
generated,\\non which view matching will also be attempted. In other 
words,\\nintroducing substitutes increases the number of invocations of 
the\\nview-matching rule. We instrumented the code to measure more\\naccurately 
the total time spent in the view-matching rule. The re-\\nsults are plotted in 
Figure 3. For 1000 views, about half of the in-\\ncrease in the optimization 
time originated in the view-matching\\ncode. When there are fewer views, fewer 
substitutes are generated\\non average and a larger fraction of the time is 
spent on view\\nmatching. When there are very few views, most invocations 
will\\nproduce no substitutes at all, in which case all of the time is 
spent\\non (unsuccessful) view matching.\\nWe also measured how effectively the 
filter tree reduces the num-\\nber of views that have to be tested more 
carefully. The view-\\nmatching rule may be invoked on expressions that are not 
accept-\\nable. The expression may contain, for example, a subquery. If 
so,\\nthe rule returns immediately without checking any views. 
These\\nunsuccessful invocations are not counted in the subsequent sum-\\nmary. 
If this first test is successful, we begin looking for accept-\\nable views. 
Without the filter tree, every view would then have to\\nbe checked. Using the 
filter tree, most views are discarded quickly\\nand only a small set of 
candidate views remain. The filter tree was\\nhighly effective on our workload; 
it consistently reduced the can-\\ndidate set to less than 0.4% of the views on 
average. The actual\\nnumbers were 0.29% for 100 views and 0.36% for 1000 
views.\\nFigure 2: Optimization time as a function of\\nthe number of 
views.\\n0\\n50\\n100\\n150\\n200\\n0\\n200\\n400\\n600\\n800\\n1000\\nNo of 
views\\nOpt. time (sec)\\nAlt & No Filter\\nNo Alt & No Filter\\nAlt & 
Filter\\nNo Alt & Filter\\nFigure 3: Total increase in optimization time\\nand 
time spent in view-matching 
rule\\n0\\n10\\n20\\n30\\n40\\n50\\n60\\n0\\n200\\n400\\n600\\n800\\n1000\\nNo 
of views\\nIncrease in optimization\\ntime\\nTotal increase\\nView matching 
time\\n340\\n\\nOn average, between 15% and 20% of the candidate views 
passed\\nthe further checking and produced substitutes. The average num-\\nber 
of substitutes produced per invocation was low: 0.04 for 100\\nviews, rising to 
0.59 for 1000 views. This means that most invo-\\ncations found no matching 
views at all. However, the view-\\nmatching rule is invoked multiple times per 
query. On our work-\\nload, the average number of invocations was between 17.8 
and\\n17.9 times. The average number of substitutes produced per query\\nranged 
from 0.7 for 100 views to 10.5 for 1000 views. In other\\nwords, on average, 
over ten different rewrites of the query using\\nviews were considered by the 
optimizer.\\nFigure 4: No of final query plans using\\nmaterialized 
views.\\n0\\n200\\n400\\n600\\n800\\n1000\\n0\\n200\\n400\\n600\\n800\\n1000\\nN
o of views\\nNo of plans\\nFigure 4 plots how many of the final execution plans 
actually used\\nmaterialized views. Considering that views and queries were 
ran-\\ndomly generated, the curve behaves as expected, namely, the\\nbenefit of 
additional views decreases with the number of views al-\\nready in the system. 
Already with 200 views, the best execution\\nplan used views for about 60% of 
the queries. With 1000 views,\\nthis increased to about 87%. All this shows is 
that view matching\\nis working, finding applicable views and producing 
improved\\nplans.\\n6. Related work\\nAlgorithms proposed in the literature for 
solving the view-\\nmatching problem differ in several respects:\\n• \\nClass of
query expressions considered.\\n• \\nClass of views supported.\\n• \\nClass of 
substitute expressions considered.\\n• \\nWhether and how multiple substitutes 
are handled.\\n• \\nWhether bag semantics or set semantics is assumed.\\n• 
\\nWhich constraints, if any, are exploited.\\nHowever, a view-matching 
algorithm solves only part of the op-\\ntimization problem. The algorithm must 
also be integrated into the\\noverall optimization process so that all valid 
rewrites using views\\nare generated and evaluated in the same way as rewrites 
not using\\nviews.\\nMany papers on query optimization using views ignore\\nthis
aspect completely, some consider only the issue of finding all\\nvalid rewrites,
and some propose selecting a best rewrite by heu-\\nristic rules.\\nLarson and 
Yang [10] were the first to describe a view-matching\\nalgorithm for SPJ queries
and views. They assumed set seman-\\ntics, considered only single-table 
substitutes and did not consider\\nconstraints. Their algorithm sometimes 
produced substitutes not\\nexpressible in SQL or standard relation 
algebra.\\nOur view-\\nmatching algorithm has much in common with the approach 
taken\\nin this paper. In a subsequent paper [17], Larson and Yang ex-\\ntended 
their algorithm to consider substitutes consisting of joins\\nof views. The 
focus was on finding all valid rewrites but the paper\\ndid not discuss how to 
select the best rewrite.\\nChaudhuri et al. [4] published the first paper on 
incorporating the\\nuse of materialized views into query optimization, in their 
case, a\\nSystem-R style optimizer using dynamic programming.\\nThey\\ndealt 
with a limited form of SPJ queries and views and assumed\\nset semantics. Their 
matching algorithm does not consider predi-\\ncate subsumption or constraints. 
They make use of a map table\\nthat specifies which subexpressions of the query 
can be substi-\\ntuted by one of the available views. The map table is 
computed\\nbefore join enumeration begins.\\nDuring join enumeration, 
re-\\nwrites of the expression under consideration are located in the\\nmap 
table and their cost estimated in the normal way. A small\\nexperimental study 
on ten queries showed a 50% increase in op-\\ntimization time with only six 
materialized views.\\nLevy, Mendelzon and Sagiv [11] studied the complexity of 
rewrit-\\ning SPJ queries using views and proved that many related prob-\\nlems 
are NP-complete. This is true even for the simplest version\\nof the problem, 
determining whether there exists a rewrite of a\\nconjunctive query with no 
built-in predicates. (In SQL terms: the\\nwhere-clause is limited to a 
conjunction of column-equality predi-\\ncates and predicates equating a column 
to a constant.)\\nGupta, Harinaryan and Quass [9] introduced a generalized 
projec-\\ntion operator that captures duplicate elimination, 
aggregation,\\ngrouping, and duplicate-preserving projection in a unified 
frame-\\nwork. The paper focuses on transformation rules for this new 
op-\\nerator. They use the transformation rules to generate expressions\\nthat 
can be rewritten using aggregation views. Substitute expres-\\nsions are limited
to single views and do not take into account sub-\\nsumption of selection 
predicates and grouping expressions.\\nSrivastava et al. [15] present a 
view-matching algorithm for SPJG\\nqueries and views. They assume bag semantics 
but do not con-\\nsider constraints. They take into account subsumption of 
selection\\npredicates, though to what extent is not clear because the 
algo-\\nrithm for testing subsumption is not fully described. In addition 
to\\nsingle-view substitutes, they also consider substitutes consisting\\nof 
unions of views (but only for SPJ views). Not surprisingly, our\\nview-matching 
algorithm is based on conditions similar to theirs.\\nHowever, they require that
the view and the expression it replaces\\nreference exactly the same tables, 
which we do not. The paper\\ndoes not discuss how to select among multiple 
rewrites nor other\\nissues related to optimizer integration. No experimental 
results are\\nprovided. Chang and Lee [3] recognized that a view can 
some-\\ntimes be used even if it contains extra tables. However, we 
cannot\\ntell from their paper what tests they apply to verify this.\\nThe 
requirement that the grouping columns of a query must be a\\nsubset of the 
grouping columns of a view is\\nsufficient but not\\nnecessary. Yan and Larson 
[16] proved that it is sufficient that the\\ngrouping columns of the view 
functionally determine the group-\\ning columns of the query. This property is 
important in OLAP\\nenvironments because lower levels in a dimension hierarchy 
func-\\ntionally determine higher levels in the hierarchy. Park, Kim and\\nLee 
[13] exploited these functional dependencies to define a par-\\ntial order 
(lattice) among OLAP queries and views. The key ob-\\nservation is that a view 
is potentially useful only if it is at the\\nsame or lower level than the query 
in the lattice. Their algorithm\\nmay produce substitutes containing unions and 
joins. They use\\nheuristic rules for selecting the best rewrite. No 
experimental re-\\nsults are reported. Our algorithm can easily be extended to 
con-\\nsider functional dependencies in a dimension hierarchy but SQL\\nServer 
does not currently have any way to specify such functional\\ndependencies. 
However, if a dimension hierarchy is implemented\\n341\\n\\nas a set of tables 
connected by foreign keys, the functional de-\\npendencies are implied by 
foreign keys and will be exploited.\\nOracle was the first commercial database 
system to support mate-\\nrialized views[2]. Views may contain joins and 
aggregation but no\\nselections. The query rewrite algorithm is briefly 
described in the\\nOracle 8i documentation. The algorithm considers replacing 
part\\nor all of a PSJG query by a materialized view. Views with extra\\ntables 
joined in through cardinality-preserving joins are consid-\\nered and functional
dependencies among levels in dimension hier-\\narchies are taken into account. 
Multiple views may be used in a\\nsubstitute expression. If multiple rewrites 
are possible, one is se-\\nlected by heuristic rules based on the size of the 
views involved.\\nThe selected rewrite is then optimized and compared to the 
best\\nplan with no materialized views.\\nNo information is provided\\nabout the
speed or scalability of the query rewrite algorithm.\\nZaharioudakis et al. [18]
describe a view-matching algorithm im-\\nplemented in DB2 UDB. The algorithm 
performs a bottom-up\\nmatching of query graphs but does not require an exact 
match.\\nCompensating nodes, for instance, selections and further 
aggrega-\\ntion, may be introduced along the way. Cardinality-preserving\\njoins
are recognized as are subsumption of selection predicates\\n(though the exact 
algorithm is not described). Complex aggrega-\\ntions (rollup, cube, grouping 
sets) are also handled. The paper\\ndoes not specify precisely what substitute 
expressions are consid-\\nered nor does it say whether multiple rewrites are 
considered. In-\\ntegration with the cost-based optimizer is not addressed and 
no\\nexperimental results are reported.\\nPottinger and Levy [14] considered the
view-matching problem\\nfor conjunctive SPJ queries and views in the context of 
data inte-\\ngration. In that context, a view describes data available from 
some\\ndata source and a query must be answered using only the set of\\nviews. 
It may not be possible to find a rewriting that is equivalent\\nto the query. 
Instead, the objective is to find a maximally-\\ncontained rewriting, which 
provides the best answer possible\\n(most rows). The rewriting consists of a 
union of combinations of\\nviews joined together, where each such combination 
contributes\\nsome rows to the result. They present a new algorithm, called 
the\\nMiniCon algorithm, and compare its performance to two 
earlier\\nalgorithms. The MiniCon algorithm reduces the number of 
view\\ncombinations by taking into account availability of join columns.\\nThat 
is, if a view V must be joined to a table T (or another view\\ncontaining T) to 
provide complete rows but the required join col-\\numns are not among its output
columns, then V can be discarded.\\nThey then show experimentally that 
exploiting this observation\\nprovides significant speedup. It is not entirely 
clear to us how to\\ntranslate their findings into a query optimization context 
because\\nof the difference in objectives.\\n7. Summary and 
extensions\\nMaterialized views can reduce query processing time very 
signifi-\\ncantly but only if the query optimizer is able to find 
applicable\\nviews quickly. We presented an efficient view-matching 
algo-\\nrithm for SPJG views and described its integrated into a 
transfor-\\nmation-based optimizer. We also presented an index 
structure,\\ncalled a filter tree, which greatly speeds up the search for 
applica-\\nble views. Experimental results obtained from an implementation\\nin 
Microsoft SQL Server showed that the algorithm is fast and\\nscales to very 
large numbers of views. With 1000 views in the\\nsystem, average optimization 
time remained as low as 0.15 sec-\\nonds per query, even though an average of 
ten different substitutes\\nusing views were generated. To the best of our 
knowledge, no\\nother algorithm has achieved this level of speed and 
scalability.\\nWe are planning to extend the algorithm to cover a broader 
class\\nof views and substitute expressions. Some extensions are 
easy,\\nrequiring only local changes. For instance, the tests applied to 
de-\\ntermine whether a view contains all rows needed by a query are\\nsomewhat 
conservative and do not fully exploit constraints. The\\ntests can be refined in
several ways. We indicated how to take into\\naccount check constraints. Support
for predicates containing ORs\\ncan also be added relatively easily. Improved 
reasoning about\\nwhen a scalar expression can be computed from other scalar 
ex-\\npressions would also be desirable.\\nSome extensions are a wee bit more 
complex. The current algo-\\nrithm produces only single-table substitutes. We 
plan to add union\\nsubstitutes and substitutes with base table backjoins to the
reper-\\ntoire. Union substitutes cover the case when all rows needed are\\nnot 
available from a single view but can be collected from 
several\\nviews.\\nOverlapping views together with SQL’s bag 
semantics\\ncomplicate the issue. If the same rows can be obtained from 
mul-\\ntiple views, we have to make sure that they appear in the result\\nwith 
the right duplication factor. Base table backjoins cover the\\ncase when a view 
contains all tables and rows needed but some\\ncolumns are missing. In that 
case, it may be worthwhile backjoin-\\ning the view to a base table to pull in 
the missing columns, espe-\\ncially of the missing columns are available from an
index.\\n8. References\\n[1] S. Agrawal, S. Chaudhuri, V. R. Narasayya:
