\\nEmail: 1astnameQhpl. hp.com \\nAbstract \\nWhile much work has addressed the 
problem of \\nmaintaining materialized views, the important ques- \\ntion of 
optimizing queries in the presence of materi- \\nalized views has not been 
resolved. In this paper, we \\nanalyze the optimization question and provide a 
com- \\nprehensive and efficient solution. Our solution has the \\ndesirable 
property that it is a simple generalization of \\nthe traditional query 
optimization algorithm. \\n1 Introduction \\nThe idea of using materialized 
views for the ben- \\nefit of improved query processing has been proposed \\nin 
the literature more than a decade ago. In this con- \\ntext, problems such as 
definition of views, composition \\nof views, maintenance of views [BC79, KP81, 
384, \\nBLT86, CW91, Rou91, GMS931 have been researched \\nbut one topic has 
been conspicuous by its absence. \\nThis concerns the problem of the judicious 
use of ma- \\nterialized views in answering a query. \\nIt may seem that 
materialized views should be used \\nto evaluate a query whenever they are 
applicable. In \\nfact, blind applications of materialized views may re- \\nsult
in significantly worse plans compared to alter- \\nnative plans that do not use 
any materialized views. \\nWhether the use of materialized views will result in 
a \\nbetter or a worse plan depends on the query and the \\nstatistical 
properties of the database. Since queries \\nare often generated using tools and
since the statisti- \\ncal property of databases are time-varying, it should 
\\nbe the responsibility of the optimizer to consider the \\nalternative 
execution plans and to make a cost-based \\ndecision whether or not to use 
materialized views to \\nanswer a given query on a given database. Such enu- 
\\nmeration of the possible alternatives by the optimizer \\n\\\'Author\\\'s 
current address: Momferatoy 71, Athens, Greece \\n+Author\\\'s current address: 
IBM Almaden Research Center, \\nSan Jose \\nmust be syntax independent and 
efficient. By syn- \\ntax independent, we mean that the set of alternatives 
\\nenumerated by the optimizer (and hence the choice \\nof the optimal execution
plan) should not depend on \\nwhether or not the query explicitly references 
materi- \\nalized views. Thus, the optimizer must be capable of \\nconsidering 
the alternatives implied by materialized \\nviews. In particular, a materialized
view may need \\nto be considered even if the view is not directly ap- 
\\nplicable (i.e., there is no subexpression in the query \\nthat syntactically 
matches the view). Also, more than \\none materialized views may be relevant for
the given \\nquery. In such cases, the optimizer must avoid incor- \\nrect 
alternatives where mutually exclusive views are \\nused together while 
considering use of mutually com- \\npatible views. \\nThe following examples 
illustrate the issues in op- \\ntimizing queries with materialized views. The 
first \\nexample emphasizes the importance of syntax inde- \\npendence and also 
shows that sometimes use of mate- \\nrialized views may result in worse plans. 
The sec- \\nond example illustrates the subtleties in syntax in- \\ndependent 
enumeration discussed above. The exam- \\nples use a database containing an 
employee relation \\nhp(name, dno, sal, age) and a department rela- \\ntion 
Dept(dno. size, loc). \\nExample 1.1 : Let there be a materialized view 
\\nExecut ive(name, b o ,  sal) that contains all em- \\nployees whose salary is
greater than 200k. Consider \\nthe query that asks for employees (and their 
depart- \\nment number) whose salary is greater than 200k and \\nwho are in the 
department with dno = 0419. If the re- \\nlation h p  has no index on dno, then 
it is better to ac- \\ncess the materialized view Executive even though the 
\\nuser presents a query which does not refer to the mate- \\nrialized view 
Executive. This example illustrates that \\nthe use of a materialized view can 
be beneficial even if \\na query does not refer to the materialized view explic-
\\nitly. On the other hand, it may be possible to obtain a \\ncheaper plan by 
not using a materialized view even if \\nthe query does reference the view 
explicitly. Consider \\n1063-6382/95 \\n$4.00 0 1995 IEEE \\n190 \\nAuthorized 
licensed use limited to: MIT. Downloaded on December 15,2025 at 23:55:25 UTC 
from IEEE Xplore.  Restrictions apply. \\n\\nthe query that asks for all 
executives in dno = 419. \\nThis query explicitly refers to the materialized 
view \\nExecutive. However, if there is an index on the dno \\nattribute of the 
relation Emp, then it may be better to \\nexpand the view definition in order to
use the index on \\ndno attribute of the relation Emp. Thus, the choice be- 
\\ntween a materialized view and a view expansion must \\nbe cost-based and 
syntax-independent. I \\nExample 1.2: The purpose of this example is to 
\\nillustrate the nature of enumeration of alternatives \\nthat arise when 
materialized views are present. Con- \\nsider the query which asks for employees
who earn \\nmore than 220k. Although the materialized view for \\nExecutive does
not syntactically match any subex- \\npression of the query, it could still be 
used to an- \\nswer the above query by retaining the selection con- \\ndition on
salary. Next, we illustrate a case of mu- \\ntually compatible use of 
materialized views. \\nCon- \\nsider the query that asks for employees who earn 
\\nmore than 200k and who have been working in de- \\npartments of s i z e  > 30
employees. If there is a ma- \\nterialized view LargeDept (dno, l o c )  
containing all \\ndepartments (with their location) where number of \\nemployees
exceed 30, then the latter may be used \\nalong with Executive to answer the 
query. Finally, \\nthere are cases where uses of two materialized views \\nare 
incompatible. Assume that a materialized view \\nLocXmp(name, s i z e ,  l o c )
is maintained that records \\nfor each employee the location of her work. If the
\\nquery asks for all employees who work in large depart- \\nments located in 
San Francisco, then each of L o c h p  \\nand LargeDept materialized views help 
generate al- \\nternative executions. But, uses of these two material- \\nized 
views are mutually exclusive, i.e., they cannot be \\nused together to answer a 
query. I \\nThe presence of materialized views and the require- \\nment of 
syntax-independent optimization has the ef- \\nfect of increasing the space of 
alternative executions \\navailable to the optimizer since the latter must con- 
\\nsider use and non-use of the materialized views. Since \\nthe query 
optimization algorithms take time exponen- \\ntial in the size of the queries, 
we must also ensure \\nthat the above enumeration of alternatives is done ef- 
\\nficiently so as to minimize the increase in optimiza- \\ntion time. 
Furthermore, we must also recognize the \\nreality that for our proposal to be 
practical and im- \\nmediately useful, it is imperative that our proposal be 
\\na generalization of the widely accepted optimization \\nalgorithm [SAC+79]. 
\\nIn this paper, we show how syntax-independent \\nenumeration of alternative 
executions can be done ef- \\nficiently. Our proposal constitutes a simple 
extension \\nto the cost-based dynamic programming algorithm \\nof [SAC+79] and 
ensures the optimality of the cho- \\nsen plan over the extended execution 
space. The sim- \\nplicity of our extension makes our solution practically 
\\nacceptable. Yet, our approach proves to be signifi- \\ncantly better than any
simple-minded solution to the \\nproblem that may be adopted (See Section 4). 
\\nFor the rest of the paper, we assume that the \\nquery as well as the 
materialized views are conjunctive \\nqueries, i.e., the Select-Project-Join 
expressions such \\nthat the Where clause consists of a conjunction of sim- 
\\nple predicates (e.g., =, <, 2 )  only. Thus, the query \\nhas no aggregates 
or group-by clause. We will use \\nthe domain-calculus notation [U11891 to 
express con- \\njunctive queries. However, our results extend to any 
\\nSelect-Project-Join queries and extensions beyond the \\nabove class are also
considered in the full version of \\nthe paper [CKPS94]. \\nThe rest of the 
paper is organized as follows. We \\nbegin with an overview of our approach. In 
Section 3, \\nwe show how the equivalent queries may be formulated \\nfrom the 
given query and the materialized views. In \\nSection 4, we present the 
algorithm for join enumera- \\ntion. We also contrast the efficiency of our 
algorithm \\nwith the existing approaches and present an experi- \\nmental 
study. In Section 5, we discuss further gener- \\nalizations of our approach. 
Section 6 mentions related \\nwork. \\n2 \\nOverview of Our Approach \\nIn 
traditional query processing systems, references \\nto views in a query are 
expanded by their definitions, \\nresulting in a query that has only base 
tables. Re- \\nlational systems that support views can do such un- \\nfolding. 
However, the presence of materialized views \\nprovide the opportunity to fold 
one or more of the \\nsubexpressions in the query into references to mate- 
\\nrialized views, thus generating additional alternatives \\nto the unfolded 
query. Therefore, we must convey to \\nthe optimizer the information that 
enables it to fold \\nthe subexpressions corresponding to the materialized 
\\nviews. \\nFor every materialized view V, we will define a one- \\nlevel rule 
as follows. The left-hand side of the one- \\nlevel rule is a conjunctive query 
(body of the view \\ndefinition) L and the right-hand side of the rule is a 
\\nsingle literal (name of the view). We represent the \\nrule as: \\nL(X,Y) - 
V(x) \\nwhere the variables x correspond to projection vari- \\n191 
\\nAuthorized licensed use limited to: MIT. Downloaded on December 15,2025 at 
23:55:25 UTC from IEEE Xplore.  Restrictions apply. \\n\\nabies for the view. 
The variables y are variables in the \\nbody of the view definition that do not 
occur among \\nprojection variables. We call these rules one-level rules 
\\nsince a literal that occurs in the right side of any of \\nthe rules 
(view-name) does not occur in any left-hand \\nside since the left-hand side may
have references to \\nonly base tables. Thus, given a set of views that are 
\\nconjunctive queries, we can generate the correspond- \\ning set of one level 
rules from the SQL view definitions. \\nOur approach to optimization in the 
presence of \\nmaterialized views has three main steps. First, the \\nquery is 
translated in the canonical unfolded form, \\nas is done in today’s relational 
systems that support \\nviews. Second, for the given query, using the one-level 
\\nrules, we identify possible ways in which one or more \\nmaterialized views 
may be used to generate alternative \\nformulations of the query. These two 
steps together \\nensure syntax independence. Finally, an efficient join 
\\nenumeration algorithm, that retrofits the System R \\nstyle join enumeration 
algorithm [SAC+79], is used to \\nensure that the costs of alternative 
formulations are \\ndetermined and the execution plan with the least cost \\nis 
selected. \\nSince the first step is routinely done in many com- \\nmercial 
relational systems, in the rest of the paper, we \\nwill focus only on the 
second and the third steps. In \\nthe next two sections, we discuss each of 
these steps: \\n0 Encode in a data-structure (MapTable) the in- \\nformation 
about queries equivalent to the given \\none (Section 3). \\n0 Generalize the 
traditional join enumeration algo- \\nrithm so that it takes into account the 
additional \\nexecution space implied by the equivalent queries \\n(Section 4). 
This is the heart of the paper. \\n3 Equivalent Queries: Generation of \\nMap Ta
bl e \\nIn this section, we discuss how we can use one-level \\nrewrite rules to
derive queries that are equivalent to \\nthe given query in the presence of 
materialized views. \\nThis problem of generating equivalent queries in the 
\\npresence of views has been studied before [YL87](also \\nsee Section 6). In 
addition to a simplified exposition of \\nthe problem for conjunctive queries, 
the novelty here is \\nin generating an implicit representation of equivalent 
\\nqueries in such a way that the join enumeration phase \\ncan exploit it. Our 
notion of equivalence of queries is \\nas in SQL standard [ISO92]. Thus, we 
assume that \\ntwo queries are equivalent if they result in the same \\nbag of 
tuples over every database. \\nIntuitively, an equivalent query is generated by 
\\nidentifying a subexpression in the query that corre- \\nsponds to the 
left-hand side of one-level rewrite rule. \\nThe subexpression is then replaced 
by the literal in the \\nright-hand side of the rule (i.e., the view name). How-
\\never, it turns out, that a straight-forward substitution \\ncould be 
incorrect. \\nExample 3.1: Consider Example 1.2. The materi- \\nalized view 
Loc-Emp is represented by the following \\nrule: \\nEmp(name, dno, sal, age), 
Dept(dno, size, loc) \\n+ Loc-Emp(name, size, loc) \\nConsider the following 
query that obtains all employ- \\nees of age less than 35 who work in San 
Francisco (SF). \\n&(name) : - Emp(name, dno, sal, age), age < 35 \\nDept(dno, 
size, SF) \\nObserve that it is possible to obtain the query Q’ \\nthrough a 
naive substitution using the rewrite rule for \\nLoc-Emp. \\n&’(name) : 
-Lot-Emp(name, size, SF), age < 35 \\nHowever, clearly, Q and Q’ are not 
equivalent queries. \\nIn particular, Q’ is unsafe. I \\nExample 3.1 makes the 
point that a syntactic sub- \\nstitution of the body of a materialized view need
not \\nresult in an equivalent query. The crux of the problem \\nin Example 3.1 
is that the naive approach of replacing \\na matching subexpression resulted in 
a query with a \\n“dangling” selection condition that refers to a variable \\nin
the subexpression that has been replaced. Besides \\nthe fact that a 
straightforward substitution may be \\nincorrect, additional substitutions may 
be applicable \\nas seen in the following example. \\nExample 3.2 The presence 
of the materialized view \\nEtecutive is represented by the following one-level 
\\nrule. \\nEmp(name, dno, sal, age), sal > 200k \\n-+ \\nExecutive(name, dno, 
sal) \\nConsider the following query which asks for employees \\nwho work in a 
department of size at least 30 and who \\nearn more than 220k. \\n&(name) : 
-Emp(name, dno, sal, age), sal > 220k \\nDept(dno, size, loc), size > 30 \\n192

no syntactic substitution for the \\nrule for Executive, since there is no 
renaming such \\nthat the literal sal > 200k in the one-level rule for the 
\\nmaterialized view Executive maps to a literal in Q. \\nHowever, the following
query Q‘ is clearly equivalent \\nto Q .  \\n&’(name) : -Executive(name, dno,

\\nconstraints. \\nIn Section 3.1, we define safe substitution that iden- 
\\ntifies equivalent queries that result due to applications \\nof one-level

construct MapTable, that \\nimplicitly stores the queries that are equivalent to
the \\ngiven query. This MapTable is subsequently used in \\nthe join 
enumeration step. \\n3.1 \\nSafe Substitution \\nEvery safe substitution 
identifies a subexpression in \\nthe given query that may be substituted by a 
materi- \\nalized view to generate an equivalent query. As Exam- \\nple 3.2 
tells us, presence of inequality constraints needs \\nto be considered in 
identifying equivalent queries. Ac- \\ncordingly, we adopt the following 
somewhat more de- \\ntailed representation of one-level rules that recognizes 
\\nexistence of inequality constraints. \\nwhere Z(x) represents a conjunction 
of inequality (and \\nmay be arithmetic) constraints that involve only the 
\\nprojection variables x of the rule. However, L(x,y) \\nmay contain variables 
y that are not projection vari- \\nables. \\nExample 3.3 : \\nConsider the 
one-level rewrite \\nrule for Executive in Example 3.2. \\nWe note \\nthat 
Z(name,dno,sal) \\nsal \\n> \\n200k and \\nL(name, dno, sal, age) \\nEmp(name, 
dno, sal, age). \\nSince Z depends only on sal, we will abbreviate refer- 
\\nence to it as Z(sa1). I \\nThe task of finding a suitable subexpression for 
sub- \\nstitution begins with renaming of variables in a rule \\nto identify 
occurrences of the left-hand side of the rule \\nin the query. Let r be a rule 
with variables V, and Q \\nbe a query with variables VQ and constants CQ. 
\\nDefinition 3.4 A valid renaming U of r with respect \\nto a query Q is a 
symbol mapping from V, to VQ \\nsubject to the following two constraints: (a) If
v E V, \\nis a projection variable, then U(.) E VQ U CQ. (b) If \\nv E V, is not
a projection variable, then u(v) E VQ \\nand U(.) # .(U’) \\nwhere U’ is any 
other variable in V,. \\nI \\nThus, only projection variables may map to con- 
\\nstants. Moreover, no two variables in the rule may \\nmap to the same 
variable in the query unless these \\ntwo variables are both projection 
variables. \\nThe \\nreader will notice the similarity between valid renam- 
\\ning and the well-known containment mapping [CM771 \\n(cf. [UllSS]). We can 
now define a notion of safe occur- \\nrence and the corresponding safe 
substitution. In the \\nfollowing definition, the symbol + stands for logical 
\\nimplication. \\nDefinition 3.5: Given a set of one-level rules R, a \\nquery 
Q has a safe occurrence of R, if for a rewrite \\nrule T E R there is a valid 
renaming of the rule T with \\nrespect to Q such that the renamed rule has the 
form \\nL(x, y),Z(x) + V(x). Furthermore, the following two \\nconditions must 
hold: \\n(1) The query Q has the form: \\nwhere each of x, y, U and v is a set 
of variables. These \\nsets may share variables except that y must be disjoint 
\\nfrom x, U and v. \\nThe safe substitution corresponding to the above safe 
\\noccurrence is: \\n(2) Z’(x) * Z(x). \\nI \\nTesting condition (2) above 
entails checking im- \\nplication between two sets of inequality constraints. 
\\nMany efficient algorithms have been proposed that can \\ntest such 
implication (See [U11891 for an algorithm). \\nFinally, note that in reality, we
retain only a subset \\nof inequality constraints in Z’, i.e., those constraints
\\nthat are not subsumed by Z. \\n193 \\nAuthorized licensed use limited to: 
MIT. Downloaded on December 15,2025 at 23:55:25 UTC from IEEE Xplore.  
Restrictions apply. \\n\\nObserve that the definition of safe occurrence en- 
\\nsures that there could be no dangling selection condi- \\ntion (unlike 
Example 3.1) when the view replaces its \\nmatching subexpression in the query. 
On the other \\nhand, the above definition does allow for a safe sub- 
\\nstitution to be possible even if there is no syntactic \\nmatch between 
left-hand side of a rule and the query \\n(as in Example 3.2). \\nExample 3.6:

size > 30 \\nI \\nQueries obtained by safe substitution are equiva- \\nlent to 
the original query in any database that stores \\nthe materialized view, 
consistent with its view defini- \\ntion. Formally, this leads to a notion of 
equivalence \\nof queries with respect to a set of rewrite rules. In the 
\\nfollowing definition, we say that a database is a a valid \\ndatabase with

each rule returns the \\nsame bag of tuples over that database. \\nDefinition 
3.7: Two queries Q and Q’ are equivalent \\nwith respect to a set of rewrite 
rule R if they result in \\nthe same bag of tuples over any valid database for 
R. \\nI \\nWe will denote such an equivalence by Q \\nQ‘. \\nIn case Q and Q’ 
are unconditionally equivalent (i.e, \\nequivalent independent of any rewrite 
rules), we de- \\nnote that by Q = &‘. \\nLemma 3.8: If Q‘ is obtained from Q b 
y  a sequence \\nof safe substitutions with respect to a set of rewrite \\nrules
R, \\nthen Q and Q are equivalent with respect to \\nR. \\nThe lemma confirms 
the soundness of safe substitu- \\ntion and is applicable for bag as well as set
semantics. \\nFor equi-join queries with bag semantics the converse \\nof the 
theorem is true as well. The latter proof exploits \\nunique properties of bag 
equivalence that are not nec- \\nessarily true of queries with set semantics. In
the full \\npaper, we generalize the above results and also con- \\nsider the 
important case of set equivalence [CKPS94]. \\nas well as queries that are not 
necessarily conjunctive \\nqueries. \\n3.2 \\nRepresentation and Enumeration of 
\\nSafe Substitutions \\nIntuitively, each safe substitution results in a new 
\\nquery, equivalent to the given one. We encode the \\nequivalent queries by 
storing the information about \\nsafe substitutions in the MapTable) data 
structure. \\nFrom the definition of safe substitution, it follows \\nthat every
safe substitution of a query Q with respect \\nto a rule L(x,y) + V ( x )  
corresponds to a renam- \\ning U for the rule. Therefore, we can encode the in- 
\\nformation about a safe substitution by the doublet \\n[u(L),u(V)]. The first 
component in the doublet is \\ncalled the deletelist and the second component in
the \\ndoublet is called the addliteral. The deletelist denotes \\nthe 
subexpression in the query that is replaced due \\nto the safe substitution U 
and the addliteral denotes \\nthe literal that replaces deletelist. Since L may 
have \\nmore than one literals, the deletelist is a set of literals. \\nHowever,
addliteral is a single literal. The algorithm \\nto construct the MapTable for a
given query is given \\nbelow. The last for loop iterates over all literals in 
the \\nquery. Its purpose is best explained in the context of \\nthe join 
enumeration algorithm, described in the next \\nsection. \\nProcedure 
MakeMapTable(Q, R) \\nbegin \\nInitialize MapTable \\nfor each rewrite rule r : 
L + V in R do \\nfor each safe substitution U from r to Q \\ndo \\nendfor 
\\nMapTable := MapTable U [u(L), u(V)] \\nendfor \\nfor each literal q E Q do 
\\nendfor \\nMapTable := MapTable U [ { q } ,  q] \\nend \\nExample 3.9: In 
addition to the rules for Executive \\nand Loc-Emp, consider the following 
one-level rewrite \\nrule for Large-Dept \\nDept(dno, size, loc), size > 30 + 
Large-Dept(dno, loc) \\n194 \\nAuthorized licensed use limited to: MIT.

for em- \\nployees who work at a department in SF. \\nQuery(name) : -Emp(name, 
dno, sal, age), size > 30 \\nDept(dno, size, SF) \\nIt can be seen that the 
MapTable will have the follow- \\ning two doublets. \\n({Dept(dno, size, SF), 
size > 30}, \\nLarge-Dept(dno, SF) \\n({Emp(name, dno, sal, age), Dept(dno,

\\nthan 30 employees. \\n&\\\'(name) : -Emp(name, dno, sal, age), sal > 200k,

\\nLarge-Dept(dno, loc)) \\nNotice that these two doublets implicit represent 
three \\nalternatives to the given query. \\nIn our implementation of the 
optimizer, literals of \\nthe query are stored in a literal-table and are refer-
\\nenced by unique literal-ids \\\'. For example, a query \\nwith four literals 
may be represented as {1,2,3,4}. \\nA MapTable entry would be of the form 
({1,2}, 7) \\nwhich indicates that the occurrence of the literal that 
\\nreplaces the subexpression corresponding to { 1,2} is \\nstored in position 7
of the literal table. For efficiency \\nof access, MapTable is indexed by 
literal-ids that occur \\nin the deletelist. \\n\\\'There is one entry in the 
literal table for each table variable \\nin SQL. The selection conditions are 
treated in a special way. \\nThese details are discussed in [CKPS94]. \\nThe 
running time of the algorithm MakeMapTable \\nis linear in the number of safe 
substitutions. However, \\nit may seem that in order to determine all safe sub- 
\\nstitutions, exhaustive renaming of the variables in the \\nrule will be 
required. Such is rarely the case [CKPS94]. \\nRather, in the likely event where
the query has at \\nmost one literal for every table name, there can be \\nat 
most one safe substitution for every rule. We note \\nthat such a safe 
substitution can be found in time \\nlinear in the size of the query. For 
example, in Exam- \\nple 3.2, we know that the literal Emp in the body of \\nthe
rule must map to the literal for E m p  in the body \\nof the query. 
Furthermore, our experience with the \\noptimizer indicates that for a given 
query, only few \\nrules are applicable. Therefore, for a given query, the 
\\nsize of MapTable is not a dominant factor and find- \\ning safe mappings is 
typically an inexpensive step in \\noptimization [CKPS94]. \\n4 \\nJoin 
Enumeration \\nIn the previous section, we have seen how the in- \\nformation 
about equivalent queries is implicitly stored \\nin MapTable. The equivalent 
queries provide the op- \\ntimizer with an extended execution space since the 
\\noptimizer can pick a plan from the union of execu- \\ntion spaces of these 
equivalent queries. Therefore, the \\nchallenge is to extend the traditional 
join enumera- \\ntion algorithm such that optimality over the extended 
\\nexecution space is ensured. \\nAn obvious solution is to invoke the 
traditional op- \\ntimizer repeatedly for each equivalent query. Indeed, \\nthis
technique was adopted in [CGMSO]. Unfortu- \\nnately, the above approach leads 
to rederivation of \\nmany shared subplans among the equivalent queries, \\nthus
leading to significant inefficiency in optimization \\n(See Section 4.3). In 
contrast, our approach guaran- \\ntees that no subplan is rederived. We show 
that while \\nthe worst case complexity of other enumeration algo- \\nrithms 
could be an exponential function of the number \\nof safe substitutions, our 
algorithm takes time only \\nlinear in the number of safe substitutions. Thus, 
not \\nonly is the enumeration algorithm a simple extension \\nof the 
traditional approach, it also is an efficient algo- \\nrithm. \\nIn the first 
part of this section, we will review the \\ntraditional join enumeration 
algorithm which is widely \\nused in relational systems. Next, we propose our 
ex- \\ntension to the existing algorithm to enumerate the \\nexpanded execution 
space. We present a result that \\nshows that the algorithm achieves complete 
enumera- \\n195 \\nAuthorized licensed use limited to: MIT. Downloaded on 
December 15,2025 at 23:55:25 UTC from IEEE Xplore.  Restrictions apply.

We contrast our \\nenumeration algorithm with known approaches. \\n4.1 
Traditional Algorithm \\nThe execution of a query is traditionally repre- 
\\nsented syntactically as annotated join trees [GHK92] \\nwhere the internal 
node is a join operation and each \\nleaf node is a base table. The annotations 
provide \\ndetails such as selection conditions, choice of access \\npaths and 
join algorithms. The set of all annotated \\njoin trees for a query that are 
considered by the op- \\ntimizer] is traditionally called the ezecution space of
\\nthe query. Like many relational optimizers, we will \\nrestrict the execution
space for each alternative to be \\nits lefl-deep trees only. Note that in such 
a case, every \\nexecution is a total ordering of joins. \\nThe optimality of a 
plan is with respect to a cost \\nmodel. So far as the cost model is concerned] 
we as- \\nsume that the cost model assigns a real number to \\nany given plan in
the execution space and satisfies the \\nprinciple of optimality [CLRSO]. \\nIn 
this part, we briefly explain the join enumeration \\nalgorithm OptPlan (See 
Figure 2), which is a simpli- \\nfication and abstraction of the algorithm in 
[SAP791 \\n(cf. [GHK92]). Let us assume that the query is a join \\namong n 
literals where n > 2. The optimal plan for \\njoin of n relations can be 
obtained by enumerating n \\nchoices for the last relation to join and for each 
choice \\njoining the chosen relation with the optimal plan for \\nthe remaining
(n-1) relations. The optimal plan is the \\nleast expensive plan of these n 
plans, so constructed. \\nWe omitted the details of the actual join methods and 
\\nother annotations of the actual execution since they \\nare not germane to 
our discussion here. Note that \\nevery subset S of the above set of all n 
relations in \\na query corresponds to a unique subquery (say, Qs). \\nThus, the
optimal plan for every subexpression QS \\nof Q (referred to as a subplan) is 
constructed exactly \\nonce and it is stored in the plantable. All subsequent 
\\ncalls to OptPlan for the same Q s  looks up the cost \\nof the optimal plan 
from the table. Since looking up \\nthe plantable helps avoid repeated 
recomputation of \\nthe optimal plan, the complexity of the algorithm is 
\\nO(n2"-\\\') (instead of O(n!)). \\n4.2 Extended Algorithm \\nIn this section,
we discuss the optimization algo- \\nrithm in the presence of equivalent queries
(implic- \\nitly) represented in the MapTable. \\nWe have pre- \\nsented the 
algorithm ExOptPlan in Figure 1. The \\ntest Di C Q tests whether all the 
literals that occur \\nin Di (a deletelist) also occurs in Q. \\nAs explained in
Section 4.1, in the traditional al- \\ngorithm, complete enumeration of the 
search space is \\nachieved by repeating the following step for each lit- 
\\neral qi in the query Q. We construct a plan for the rest \\nof the literals 
in the query, i.e., the optimal plan for \\nQ - qi. Putting together the optimal
plan for (Q - q,) \\nwith q; results in the optimal plan for Q subject to the 
\\nrestriction that qi is the last literal being joined. \\nOur extension 
follows the above technique for enu- \\nmeration closely. Recall that MapTable 
contains the \\ndoublets ({qi},qi) for each literal qi that is in the \\nquery 
Q. It can be seen immediately that if these are \\nthe only doublets stored in 
MapTable, the algorithms \\nOptPlan and ExOptPlan in Figure 1 behave identi- 
\\n196 \\nAuthorized licensed use limited to: MIT. Downloaded on December 
15,2025 at 23:55:25 UTC from IEEE Xplore.  Restrictions apply. \\n\\ncally since
Pi in ExOptPlan will be no different from \\nSi in OptPlan. Let us now consider 
any other doublet \\nthat corresponds to a safe substitution. A key obser- 
\\nvation is that we can ensure exhaustive enumeration if \\nfor each such safe 
substitution (D;, ai), we consider all \\nplans where a; is the last literal to 
be joined. However, \\nin the unfolded query, there is no occurrence of the ma- 
\\nterialized view ai. Therefore, instead of constructing \\nthe plan (Q - a;) W
a,, we must construct the optimal \\nplan for (Q - 0,) W a;, since D; is the set
of literals \\n(i.e., subexpression) in the unfolded query which when 
\\nreplaced by a; results in an equivalent query. As can \\nbe seen, our 
algorithm does precisely the above. \\nCorrectness and Complexity \\nThe 
execution space over which the optimal plan for \\nthe query Q (with respect to 
a set R of one-level \\nrewrite rules) is being sought is the set of all 
left-deep \\ntrees over the queries that are obtained from Q by \\nsafe 
substitution with respect to R. The optimization \\nproblem is to pick an 
optimal plan from the above exe- \\ncution space with respect to a cost model 
that respects \\nthe principle of optimality. \\nTheorem 4.1 : \\nthe optimal 
plan with respect to a given MapTable. \\nThe algorithm ExOptPlan produces 
\\nObserve that a step which we need to perform effi- \\nciently in 
E;cOptPlan(Q) is to check if deletelist \\nQ. \\nIn order to do so, we use bit 
maskings to represent \\nthe literals in deletelist and the subquery Q. Then, 
\\nthe subset relationship can be checked with bit-wise \\nlogical operators in 
O( 1) in most cases. \\nIn the absence of any equivalent queries, the time 
\\ncomplexity of ExOptPlan is no different from Opt- \\nPlan, the traditional 
join enumeration algorithm used \\nby commercial optimizers. Therefore, the 
interest- \\ning complexity question is the dependence of the time \\ncomplexity
of ExOptPlan on the number of safe sub- \\nstitutions in MapTable (say, 1) for 
the query Q. It \\ncan be shown that the time complexity of ExOptPlan \\nis 
bounded by O(12") (when computed in a generous \\nmatter). In contrast, the time
complexity for Opt- \\nPlan is O(n2"-\\\'). Thus, the worst case complexity 
\\ndegrades by at most 21/n. As argued in Section 3.2, \\nthe number of safe 
substitutions ( I )  is likely to be small \\nand so the relative increase in 
optimization time is \\nvery modest. \\nWe can complement the discussion of

by the optimizer. We have proved the following \\nproperty of ExOptPlan, which 
says that ExOptPlan \\navoids generation of redundant plans. A detailed dis-

ExOptPlan stores a unique optimal subplan. \\nComparison to Other Approaches 
\\nThe simplest alternative to ExOptPlan is to invoke the \\noptimizer for each 
equivalent query. This approach \\nturns out to be very inefficient. Let us 
assume that \\na query is of size n and the MapTable has 1 entries. \\nThen, the
worst case time complexity of the simple \\napproach is O(lnn2"-\\\'), which is 
significantly worse \\nthan the upper bound for ExOptPlan, which is O(12"). 
\\nIntuitively, the folly of the naive approach is that no \\nsubplans are 
reused and all shared plans are rederived. \\nThe need for sharing plans for the
subqueries was ob- \\nserved in [CS93, CR941. In that approach, optimal \\nplans
of the shared subqueries are maintained and \\nreused. \\nHowever, while this 
approach maintains a \\nunique optimal subplan for each shared subquery, it 
\\ndoes not maintain a unique optimal plan for each set \\nof equivalent 
subqueries [CKPS94]. \\n4.3 An Experimental Study \\nOur complexity analysis 
shows that the increase in \\noptimization cost is modest compared to 
traditional \\noptimization. To strengthen our confidence, we used \\nour 
implementation of the optimizer for experiment- \\ning, which seems to point to 
the computational effi- \\nciency of our algorithm as well. \\nOur optimization 
algorithm was executed on ten \\nqueries consisting of seven relations and six 
equality \\n197 \\nAuthorized licensed use limited to: MIT. Downloaded on 
December 15,2025 at 23:55:25 UTC from IEEE Xplore.  Restrictions apply. 
\\n\\njoins. Among all relations participating in the query, \\n50% of relations
were chosen and an attribute of each \\nselected relation was assigned to have 
selection predi- \\ncate with equality predicate. These attributes for se- 
\\nlection condition were chosen among those who did \\nnot participate in join 
predicates. \\nFor each query, \\nsix views were generated as the same as joins 
in the \\nquery and projection attributes of views were selected \\nso that the 
materialized views can be used to gener- \\nate equivalent queries. We tested 
each query vary- \\ning the number of materialized views available rang- \\ning 
from 0 to 6. Note that due to the presence of \\nindexes, the decision of using 
(and selecting) materi- \\nalized views had to be based on cost estimates. We 
\\nhave used an experimental framework similar to that \\nin [IK90, INSS92, 
Kan91, Shi501. The details are \\nin [CKPS94]. \\nThe experimental result is 
shown in Figure 2. The \\ncost of optimization is normalized with respect to the
\\ncost of optimizing a single query, as in the traditional \\noptimizer. The 
effect of saving redundant work by \\nour enumeration algorithm has resulted in 
a rather \\nslow growth in optimization cost. In particular, for \\nthe case 
where there are 16 equivalent queries, the \\nadditional optimization cost on 
the average was less \\nthan 50%. \\n5 \\nDiscussion \\nIn the introduction, we 
stated that we would like \\nthe optimization to be syntax independent and effi-
\\ncient. Let us revisit those desiderata to see whether \\nour optimization 
algorithm ensures that these require- \\nments are satisfied. \\nObserve that 
syntax independent optimization is \\nachieved because we unfold all the queries
in terms \\nof base tables to provide a canonical representation of \\nthe 
query. Opportunities for using materialized views \\nare then discovered by 
enumerating safe substitutions \\nusing one-level rules. Furthermore, our 
enumeration \\nalgorithm is also capable of deciding amongst the use \\nof 
multiple materialized views when their uses are mu- \\ntually exclusive (i.e., 
use of one view excludes the use \\nof another). \\nExample 5.1: Let us assume 
that the query Q is \\n{1,2,3,4,5,6). Let the entries in the MapTable be \\nthe 
following three doublets: \\nObserve that because of the enumeration strategy in
\\nExOptPlan, any candidate plan P that uses the lit- \\neral 7 (an occurrence 
of a materialized view), ensures \\nthat in the (unfolded) query corresponding 
to the rest \\nof the plan, subexpression {1,2) is absent, since they 
\\noccurred in the deletelist of 7 in the MapTable. There- \\nfore, the rest of 
the plan P can not have an occurrence \\nof the other “overlapping” materialized
view 8 since \\nthe subexpression for 2 will be missing. On the other \\nhand, 
the cost of a plan which uses both the views 7 \\nand 9 will be considered. For 
simplicity, consider a \\nplan where the literal 7 occurs as the last literal to
\\nbe joined. The remainder of the plan (i.e., excluding \\nthe literal 7) 
represents the subexpression {3,4,5,6). \\nTherefore, while optimizing 
recursively, the plan for \\n{3,6,9} will be considered. I \\nLast but not the 
least, our objective was to ensure \\nthat the extensions to the optimizer are 
simple. A \\ncomparison of Figures 2 and Figures 3 confirms that \\nthis goal 
has been met. \\nGeneralizations \\nThe enumeration algorithm ExOptPlan is 
robust \\nin that it is completely independent of the algorithm \\nused to 
generate MapTable. This would make it pos- \\nsible to pick an algorithm for 
generating equivalent \\nqueries using other algorithms [Fin82, YL87, CR94] 
\\n(see discussion in the related work). We have also \\nstudied several 
important extensions to the algorithm \\nto efficiently handle nested views and 
to avoid applica- \\ntions of one-level rules where unnecessary. These are 
\\ndescribed in the full paper [CKPS94]. \\nThe optimization algorithm presented
in this paper \\nextends to the case where the query and the material- \\nized 
views are single block Select-Project-Join queries \\n(i.e., not necessarily 
conjunctive queries). For arbi- \\ntrary SQL queries, the extensions are more 
complex. \\nFor the case where the query and the views are arbi- \\ntrary 
first-order queries, enumeration cannot be com- \\nplete since we have shown in 
the full paper that given \\na set of relational views S and two relational 
queries \\nQ & Q‘, it is undecidable whether Q ES Q’. \\nFinally, note that our 
algorithm can be used to ex- \\nploit cached results. Caching results of a query
to \\nspeed up query processing has been suggested in ad- \\nvanced database 
management systems such as Post- \\ngres. We observe that the cached results of 
queries \\ndiffer from materialized views in that there may not be \\nany degree
of permanence to the cache. For optimiz- \\ning an interactive query, it may be 
profitable to exploit \\nthe results that are currently cached. However, as in 
\\nthe case of materialized views, such choices need to \\nbe cost-based. 
Therefore, we maintain a system table \\n198 \\nAuthorized licensed use limited 
to: MIT. Downloaded on December 15,2025 at 23:55:25 UTC from IEEE Xplore.  
Restrictions apply. \\n\\nwhich records the queries (in unfolded form) that are 
\\ncached and their corresponding one-level rules. This \\ntable is updated by 
the cache manager to reflect the \\ncontents of the current cache. \\n6 Related 
Work \\nTo the best of our knowledge, no work has \\npreviously been done on 
extending the dynamic- \\nprogramming based join enumeration algorithm to 
\\noptimize queries in a cost-based fashion when the \\ndatabase contains one or
more materialized views. \\nFor example, although Postgres [SJGPSO] provides the
\\nability to implement a view either through material- \\nization or by view 
expansion, the choice between the \\napproaches has to be predetermined. Thus, 
the opti- \\nmizer can not explore both the options depending on \\nthe query 
and cost estimations. \\nThe task of generating equivalent queries based \\non 
existing query fragments or semantic knowledge \\nhas been studied in several 
different contexts [Fin82, \\nLY85, YL87, Se188, CGM90, CS93, CR94]. However, 
\\nall these techniques generate equivalent queries explic- \\nitly. In 
contrast, much of our efficiency in optimiza- \\ntion stems from the implicit 
encoding of the set of \\nequivalent queries in MapTable and a join enumera- 
\\ntion algorithm that exploits the encoding. \\n7 Summary \\nWe have presented 
a comprehensive approach to \\nsolving the problem of optimization in the 
presence of \\nmaterialized views. Our solution is not only efficient \\nbut is 
also syntax independent and cost-based. Ev- \\nery materialized view corresponds
to a one-level rule. \\nThe set of equivalent queries due to applications of the
\\nabove rules are encoded compactly in the MapTable \\ndata structure. This 
data structure is used by the enu- \\nmeration algorithm to efficiently 
enumerate the the \\nspace of additional execution alternatives generated \\ndue
to one-level rules (i.e., due to presence of mate- \\nrialized views). Our 
proposal requires few extensions \\nto the traditional optimization algorithm 
that is used \\nby commercial systems. Our approach also extends to 
\\narchitectures where results of queries are cached. \\nReferences \\n[BC79]
